<p><b>Thomas Jefferson</b> (April 13 [<a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">O.S.</a> April 2]&#160;1743&#160;– July 4, 1826) was an American <a href="/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States" title="Founding Fathers of the United States">Founding Father</a> who was the principal author of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a> and later served as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">third President of the United States</a> from 1801 to 1809. Previously, he was <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1796" title="United States presidential election, 1796">elected</a> the second <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">Vice President of the United States</a>, serving under <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a> from 1797 to 1801. A proponent of democracy, <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">republicanism</a>, and individual rights motivating <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">American colonists</a> to break from Great Britain and form a new nation, he produced formative documents and decisions at both the state and national level.</p>
<p>Jefferson was primarily of English ancestry, born and educated in <a href="/wiki/Colony_of_Virginia" title="Colony of Virginia">colonial Virginia</a>. He graduated from the <a href="/wiki/College_of_William_%26_Mary" title="College of William &amp; Mary">College of William &amp; Mary</a> and briefly practiced law, at times defending slaves seeking their freedom. During the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>, he represented Virginia in the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Congress" title="Continental Congress">Continental Congress</a> that adopted the Declaration, drafted the law for religious freedom as a Virginia legislator, and he served as a wartime governor (1779–1781). He became the United States Minister to France in May 1785, and subsequently the nation's first <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a> in 1790–1793 under President George Washington. Jefferson and <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a> organized the <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican Party</a> to oppose the <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist Party</a> during the formation of the <a href="/wiki/First_Party_System" title="First Party System">First Party System</a>. With Madison, he anonymously wrote the controversial <a href="/wiki/Kentucky_and_Virginia_Resolutions" title="Kentucky and Virginia Resolutions">Kentucky and Virginia Resolutions</a> in 1798–1799, which sought to embolden <a href="/wiki/States%27_rights" title="States' rights">states' rights</a> in opposition to the national government by nullifying the <a href="/wiki/Alien_and_Sedition_Acts" title="Alien and Sedition Acts">Alien and Sedition Acts</a>.</p>
<p>As President, Jefferson pursued the nation's shipping and trade interests against <a href="/wiki/Barbary_pirates" title="Barbary pirates">Barbary pirates</a> and aggressive British trade policies. He also organized the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</a>, almost doubling the country's territory. As a result of peace negotiations with France, his administration reduced military forces. He was <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1804" title="United States presidential election, 1804">reelected in 1804</a>. Jefferson's second term was beset with difficulties at home, including the trial of former Vice President <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Burr" title="Aaron Burr">Aaron Burr</a>. American foreign trade was diminished when Jefferson implemented the <a href="/wiki/Embargo_Act_of_1807" title="Embargo Act of 1807">Embargo Act of 1807</a>, responding to British threats to U.S. shipping. In 1803, Jefferson began a controversial process of Indian tribe removal to the newly organized <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Territory" title="Louisiana Territory">Louisiana Territory</a>, and he signed the <a href="/wiki/Act_Prohibiting_Importation_of_Slaves" title="Act Prohibiting Importation of Slaves">Act Prohibiting Importation of Slaves</a> in 1807.</p>
<p>Jefferson mastered many disciplines, which ranged from surveying and mathematics to horticulture and mechanics. He was a proven architect in the <a href="/wiki/Classical_architecture" title="Classical architecture">classical tradition</a>. Jefferson's keen interest in religion and philosophy earned him the presidency of the <a href="/wiki/American_Philosophical_Society" title="American Philosophical Society">American Philosophical Society</a>. He shunned organized religion, but was influenced by both Christianity and deism. He was well versed in linguistics and spoke several languages. He founded the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia" title="University of Virginia">University of Virginia</a> after retiring from public office. He was a prolific letter writer and corresponded with many prominent and important people throughout his adult life. His only full-length book is <i><a href="/wiki/Notes_on_the_State_of_Virginia" title="Notes on the State of Virginia">Notes on the State of Virginia</a></i> (1785), considered the most important American book published before 1800.</p>
<p>Jefferson owned several plantations which were worked by hundreds of slaves. Most historians now believe that, after the death of his wife in 1782, he had a relationship with his slave <a href="/wiki/Sally_Hemings" title="Sally Hemings">Sally Hemings</a> and fathered at least one of her children. Historians have lauded Jefferson's public life, noting his primary authorship of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a> during the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">Revolutionary War</a>, his advocacy of religious freedom and tolerance in Virginia, and the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</a> while he was president. Various modern scholars are more critical of Jefferson's private life, pointing out the discrepancy between his ownership of slaves and his liberal political principles, for example. Presidential scholars, however, consistently <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Historical rankings of Presidents of the United States">rank Jefferson</a> among the greatest presidents.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_career">Early life and career</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Early_life_and_career_of_Thomas_Jefferson" title="Early life and career of Thomas Jefferson">Early life and career of Thomas Jefferson</a></div>
<p>Thomas Jefferson was born on April 13, 1743 (April 2, 1743 <a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">Old Style</a>, <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian calendar</a>), at the family home in <a href="/wiki/Shadwell_(Virginia)" class="mw-redirect" title="Shadwell (Virginia)">Shadwell</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Colony_of_Virginia" title="Colony of Virginia">Colony of Virginia</a>, the third of ten children.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> He was of English and possibly Welsh descent and was born a British subject.<sup id="cite_ref-Malone5_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Malone5-2">[2]</a></sup> His father <a href="/wiki/Peter_Jefferson" title="Peter Jefferson">Peter Jefferson</a> was a planter and surveyor who died when Jefferson was fourteen; his mother was <a href="/wiki/Jane_Randolph_Jefferson" title="Jane Randolph Jefferson">Jane Randolph</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[a]</a></sup> Peter Jefferson moved his family to <a href="/wiki/Tuckahoe_Plantation" class="mw-redirect" title="Tuckahoe Plantation">Tuckahoe Plantation</a> in 1745 upon the death of a friend who had named him guardian of his children. The Jeffersons returned to Shadwell in 1752, where Peter died in 1757; his estate was divided between his sons Thomas and <a href="/wiki/Randolph_Jefferson" title="Randolph Jefferson">Randolph</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup> Thomas inherited approximately 5,000 acres (2,000&#160;ha; 7.8&#160;sq&#160;mi) of land, including <a href="/wiki/Monticello" title="Monticello">Monticello</a>. He assumed full authority over his property at age 21.<sup id="cite_ref-Malone437_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Malone437-6">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rear_view_of_the_Wren_Building,_College_of_William_%26_Mary_in_Williamsburg,_Virginia,_USA_(2008-04-23).jpg" class="image"><img alt="A university building" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Rear_view_of_the_Wren_Building%2C_College_of_William_%26_Mary_in_Williamsburg%2C_Virginia%2C_USA_%282008-04-23%29.jpg/220px-Rear_view_of_the_Wren_Building%2C_College_of_William_%26_Mary_in_Williamsburg%2C_Virginia%2C_USA_%282008-04-23%29.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Rear_view_of_the_Wren_Building%2C_College_of_William_%26_Mary_in_Williamsburg%2C_Virginia%2C_USA_%282008-04-23%29.jpg/330px-Rear_view_of_the_Wren_Building%2C_College_of_William_%26_Mary_in_Williamsburg%2C_Virginia%2C_USA_%282008-04-23%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Rear_view_of_the_Wren_Building%2C_College_of_William_%26_Mary_in_Williamsburg%2C_Virginia%2C_USA_%282008-04-23%29.jpg/440px-Rear_view_of_the_Wren_Building%2C_College_of_William_%26_Mary_in_Williamsburg%2C_Virginia%2C_USA_%282008-04-23%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rear_view_of_the_Wren_Building,_College_of_William_%26_Mary_in_Williamsburg,_Virginia,_USA_(2008-04-23).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wren Building (rear), College of William &amp; Mary where Jefferson studied</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jefferson began his childhood education beside the <a href="/wiki/Randolph_family_of_Virginia" title="Randolph family of Virginia">Randolph</a> children with tutors at <a href="/wiki/Tuckahoe,_Virginia" title="Tuckahoe, Virginia">Tuckahoe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup> In 1752, he began attending a local school run by a Scottish Presbyterian minister. At age nine, he started studying the natural world as well as three languages: Latin, Greek, and French. By this time he also learned to ride horses. He was taught from 1758 to 1760 by Reverend <a href="/wiki/James_Maury" title="James Maury">James Maury</a> near <a href="/wiki/Gordonsville,_Virginia" title="Gordonsville, Virginia">Gordonsville, Virginia</a>, where he studied history, science, and the classics while boarding with Maury's family.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson entered the <a href="/wiki/College_of_William_%26_Mary" title="College of William &amp; Mary">College of William &amp; Mary</a> in <a href="/wiki/Williamsburg,_Virginia" title="Williamsburg, Virginia">Williamsburg, Virginia</a>, at age 16 and studied mathematics, metaphysics, and philosophy under Professor <a href="/wiki/William_Small" title="William Small">William Small</a>. Small introduced him to the <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">British Empiricists</a> including <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>. Jefferson improved his French and Greek and his skill at the violin. He graduated two years after starting in 1762. He <a href="/wiki/Reading_law" title="Reading law">read the law</a> under Professor <a href="/wiki/George_Wythe" title="George Wythe">George Wythe</a>'s tutelage to obtain his law license, while working as a <a href="/wiki/Law_clerk" title="Law clerk">law clerk</a> in Wythe's office.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup> He also read a wide variety of English classics and political works.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson treasured his books. In 1770, his Shadwell home was destroyed by fire, including a library of 200 volumes inherited from his father.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> Nevertheless, he had replenished his library with 1,250 titles by 1773, and his collection grew to almost 6,500 volumes in 1814.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> The British burned the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a> that year, and he sold more than 6,000 books to the Library for $23,950. He had intended to pay off some of his large debt, but he resumed collecting for his personal library, writing to <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a>, "I cannot live without books".<sup id="cite_ref-library_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-library-13">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lawyer_and_House_of_Burgesses">Lawyer and House of Burgesses</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:House_of_Burgesses_in_the_Capitol_Williamsburg_James_City_County_Virginia_by_Frances_Benjamin_Johnston.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Chamber of House of Burgesses" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/House_of_Burgesses_in_the_Capitol_Williamsburg_James_City_County_Virginia_by_Frances_Benjamin_Johnston.jpg/220px-House_of_Burgesses_in_the_Capitol_Williamsburg_James_City_County_Virginia_by_Frances_Benjamin_Johnston.jpg" width="220" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/House_of_Burgesses_in_the_Capitol_Williamsburg_James_City_County_Virginia_by_Frances_Benjamin_Johnston.jpg/330px-House_of_Burgesses_in_the_Capitol_Williamsburg_James_City_County_Virginia_by_Frances_Benjamin_Johnston.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/House_of_Burgesses_in_the_Capitol_Williamsburg_James_City_County_Virginia_by_Frances_Benjamin_Johnston.jpg/440px-House_of_Burgesses_in_the_Capitol_Williamsburg_James_City_County_Virginia_by_Frances_Benjamin_Johnston.jpg 2x" data-file-width="971" data-file-height="763" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:House_of_Burgesses_in_the_Capitol_Williamsburg_James_City_County_Virginia_by_Frances_Benjamin_Johnston.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
House of Burgesses in Williamsburg, Virginia, where Jefferson served 1769–1775</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jefferson was admitted to the Virginia bar in 1767 and then lived with his mother at Shadwell.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> In addition to practicing law, Jefferson represented <a href="/wiki/Albemarle_County,_Virginia" title="Albemarle County, Virginia">Albemarle County</a> as a delegate in the Virginia <a href="/wiki/House_of_Burgesses" title="House of Burgesses">House of Burgesses</a> from 1769 until 1775.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> He pursued reforms to slavery. He introduced legislation in 1769 allowing masters to take control over the emancipation of slaves, taking discretion away from the royal Governor and General Court. He persuaded his cousin <a href="/wiki/Richard_Bland" title="Richard Bland">Richard Bland</a> to spearhead the legislation's passage, but reaction was strongly negative.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson took seven cases for freedom-seeking slaves<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> and waived his fee for one client, who claimed that he should be freed before the statutory age of thirty-one required for emancipation in cases with inter-racial grandparents.<sup id="cite_ref-Gordon99_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gordon99-18">[17]</a></sup> He invoked <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">Natural Law</a> to argue, "everyone comes into the world with a right to his own person and using it at his own will&#160;... This is what is called personal liberty, and is given him by the author of nature, because it is necessary for his own sustenance." The judge cut him off and ruled against his client. As a consolation, Jefferson gave his client some money, conceivably used to aid his escape shortly thereafter.<sup id="cite_ref-Gordon99_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gordon99-18">[17]</a></sup> He later incorporated this sentiment into the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> He also took on 68 cases for the General Court of Virginia in 1767, in addition to three notable cases: <i>Howell v. Netherland</i> (1770), <i>Bolling v. Bolling</i> (1771), and <i>Blair v. Blair</i> (1772).<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_Time_Line_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig_Time_Line-20">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>The British Parliament passed the <a href="/wiki/Intolerable_Acts" title="Intolerable Acts">Intolerable Acts</a> in 1774, and Jefferson wrote a resolution calling for a "Day of Fasting and Prayer" in protest, as well as a boycott of all British goods. His resolution was later expanded into <i><a href="/wiki/A_Summary_View_of_the_Rights_of_British_America" title="A Summary View of the Rights of British America">A Summary View of the Rights of British America</a></i>, in which he argued that people have the right to <a href="/wiki/Self-governing_colony" title="Self-governing colony">govern themselves</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monticello.2C_marriage_and_family">Monticello, marriage and family</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Monticello_2010-10-29.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Monticello plantation house" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Monticello_2010-10-29.jpg/220px-Monticello_2010-10-29.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Monticello_2010-10-29.jpg/330px-Monticello_2010-10-29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Monticello_2010-10-29.jpg/440px-Monticello_2010-10-29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Monticello_2010-10-29.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jefferson's home Monticello</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1768, Jefferson began constructing his primary residence Monticello (Italian for "Little Mountain") on a hilltop overlooking his 5,000-acre plantation.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[b]</a></sup> Construction was done mostly by local masons and carpenters, assisted by Jefferson's slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>He moved into the South Pavilion in 1770. Turning Monticello into a neoclassical masterpiece in the <a href="/wiki/Palladian_architecture" title="Palladian architecture">Palladian</a> style was his perennial project.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>On January 1, 1772, Jefferson married his third cousin Martha Wayles Skelton, the 23-year-old widow of Bathurst Skelton, and she moved into the South Pavilion.<sup id="cite_ref-Tucker_p.47_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tucker_p.47-26">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup> She was a frequent hostess for Jefferson and managed the large household. Biographer <a href="/wiki/Dumas_Malone" title="Dumas Malone">Dumas Malone</a> described the marriage as the happiest period of Jefferson's life.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup> Martha read widely, did fine needlework, and was a skilled pianist; Jefferson often accompanied her on the violin or cello.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup> During their ten years of marriage, Martha bore six children: <a href="/wiki/Martha_Jefferson_Randolph" title="Martha Jefferson Randolph">Martha</a> "Patsy" (1772–1836); Jane (1774–1775); a son who lived for only a few weeks in 1777; <a href="/wiki/Mary_Jefferson_Eppes" title="Mary Jefferson Eppes">Mary Wayles</a> "Polly" (1778–1804); Lucy Elizabeth (1780–1781); and another Lucy Elizabeth (1782–1785). Only Martha and Mary survived more than a few years.<sup id="cite_ref-Martha_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martha-30">[28]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Martha_Jefferson_Randolph_portrait.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Martha_Jefferson_Randolph_portrait.jpg/180px-Martha_Jefferson_Randolph_portrait.jpg" width="180" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Martha_Jefferson_Randolph_portrait.jpg/270px-Martha_Jefferson_Randolph_portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Martha_Jefferson_Randolph_portrait.jpg/360px-Martha_Jefferson_Randolph_portrait.jpg 2x" data-file-width="620" data-file-height="720" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Martha_Jefferson_Randolph_portrait.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Martha_Jefferson_Randolph" title="Martha Jefferson Randolph">Jefferson's daughter Martha</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Martha's father John Wayles died in 1773, and the couple inherited 135 slaves, 11,000 acres (4,500&#160;ha; 17&#160;sq&#160;mi), and the estate's debts. The debts took Jefferson years to satisfy, contributing to his financial problems.<sup id="cite_ref-Tucker_p.47_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tucker_p.47-26">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Martha later suffered from ill health, including diabetes, and frequent childbirth further weakened her. Her mother had died young, and Martha lived with two stepmothers as a girl. A few months after the birth of her last child, she died on September 6, 1782, at the age of 33 with Jefferson at her bedside. Shortly before her death, Martha made Jefferson promise never to marry again, telling him that she could not bear to have another mother raise her children.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[29]</a></sup> Jefferson was grief-stricken by her death, relentlessly pacing back and forth, nearly to the point of exhaustion. He emerged after three weeks, taking long rambling rides on secluded roads with his daughter Martha, by her description "a solitary witness to many a violent burst of grief".<sup id="cite_ref-Martha_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martha-30">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Halliday48_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halliday48-32">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>After working as Secretary of State (1790–93), he returned to Monticello and initiated a remodeling based on the architectural concepts which he had acquired in Europe. The work continued throughout most of his presidency, being finished in 1809.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_career_1775.E2.80.931800">Political career 1775–1800</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Declaration_of_Independence">Declaration of Independence</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:United_States_Declaration_of_Independence.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Declaration of Independence" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence.jpg/220px-United_States_Declaration_of_Independence.jpg" width="220" height="261" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence.jpg/330px-United_States_Declaration_of_Independence.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence.jpg/440px-United_States_Declaration_of_Independence.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3923" data-file-height="4656" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:United_States_Declaration_of_Independence.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
U.S. Declaration of Independence – 1823 facsimile of the engrossed copy</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">United States Declaration of Independence</a></div>
<p>Jefferson was the primary author of the Declaration of Independence. At age 33, he was one of the youngest delegates to the <a href="/wiki/Second_Continental_Congress" title="Second Continental Congress">Second Continental Congress</a> beginning in 1775 at the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>, where a formal declaration of independence from Britain was overwhelmingly favored.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup> Jefferson chose his words for the Declaration in June 1775, shortly after the war had begun, where the idea of Independence from Britain had long since become popular among the colonies. He was inspired by the Enlightenment ideals of the sanctity of the individual, as well as by the writings of Locke and Montesquieu.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>He sought out John Adams, an emerging leader of the Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup> They became close friends and Adams supported Jefferson's appointment to the <a href="/wiki/Committee_of_Five" title="Committee of Five">Committee of Five</a> formed to draft a declaration of independence in furtherance of the <a href="/wiki/Lee_Resolution" title="Lee Resolution">Lee Resolution</a> passed by the Congress, which declared the United Colonies independent. The committee initially thought that Adams should write the document, but Adams persuaded the committee to choose Jefferson.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[c]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson consulted with other committee members over the next seventeen days, and drew on his own proposed draft of the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Virginia" title="Constitution of Virginia">Virginia Constitution</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Mason" title="George Mason">George Mason</a>'s draft of the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Declaration_of_Rights" title="Virginia Declaration of Rights">Virginia Declaration of Rights</a>, and other sources.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[37]</a></sup> The other committee members made some changes, and a final draft was presented to the Congress on June 28, 1776.<sup id="cite_ref-Meacham.2C_2012.2C_p._105_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham.2C_2012.2C_p._105-41">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>The declaration was introduced on Friday, June 28, and congress began debate over its contents on Monday, July 1,<sup id="cite_ref-Meacham.2C_2012.2C_p._105_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham.2C_2012.2C_p._105-41">[38]</a></sup> resulting in the omission of a fourth of the text, including a passage critical of King George III and the slave trade.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis.2C_1996.2C_p._50_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis.2C_1996.2C_p._50-42">[39]</a></sup> Jefferson resented the changes, but he did not speak publicly about the revisions.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[d]</a></sup> On July 4, 1776, the Congress <a href="/wiki/Ratification#United_States" title="Ratification">ratified</a> the Declaration, and delegates signed it on August 2; in doing so, they were committing an act of treason against the Crown.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[41]</a></sup> Jefferson's preamble is regarded as an enduring statement of human rights, and the phrase "<a href="/wiki/All_men_are_created_equal" title="All men are created equal">all men are created equal</a>" has been called "one of the best-known sentences in the English language" containing "the most potent and consequential words in American history".<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis.2C_1996.2C_p._50_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis.2C_1996.2C_p._50-42">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Virginia_state_legislator_and_governor">Virginia state legislator and governor</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:290px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Governor's Palace" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg/288px-Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg" width="288" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg/432px-Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg/576px-Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1520" data-file-height="800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Governor%27s_Palace_(Williamsburg,_Virginia)" title="Governor's Palace (Williamsburg, Virginia)">Governor's Palace</a>, Governor Jefferson's residence in Williamsburg</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At the start of the Revolution, Jefferson was a <a href="/wiki/Colonel_(United_States)" title="Colonel (United States)">Colonel</a> and was named commander of the <a href="/wiki/Albemarle_County,_Virginia" title="Albemarle County, Virginia">Albemarle County</a> Militia on September 26, 1775.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[43]</a></sup> He was then elected to the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_House_of_Delegates" title="Virginia House of Delegates">Virginia House of Delegates</a> for Albemarle County in September 1776, when finalizing a state constitution was a priority.<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson_pp._101_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson_pp._101-48">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fer26_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fer26-49">[45]</a></sup> For nearly three years, he assisted with the constitution and was especially proud of his Bill for Establishing Religious Freedom, which forbade state support of religious institutions or enforcement of religious doctrine.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[46]</a></sup> The bill failed to pass, as did his legislation to disestablish the Anglican church, but both were later revived by <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1778, Jefferson was given the task of revising the state's laws. He drafted 126 bills in three years, including laws to streamline the judicial system. Jefferson's proposed statutes provided for general education, which he considered the basis of "republican government".<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson_pp._101_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson_pp._101-48">[44]</a></sup> He had become alarmed that Virginia's powerful landed gentry were becoming a hereditary aristocracy. He took the lead in abolishing what he called "feudal and unnatural distinctions." He targeted laws such as <a href="/wiki/Entail" class="mw-redirect" title="Entail">entail</a> and <a href="/wiki/Primogeniture" title="Primogeniture">primogeniture</a> by which the oldest son inherited all the land. The <a href="/wiki/Fee_tail" title="Fee tail">entail</a> laws made it perpetual: the one who inherited the land could not sell it, but had to bequeath it to his oldest son. As a result, increasingly large plantations, worked by white tenant farmers and by black slaves, gained in size and wealth and political power in the eastern ("Tidewater") tobacco areas.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[48]</a></sup> During the Revolutionary era, all such laws were repealed by the states that had them.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson was elected <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Virginia" title="Governor of Virginia">governor</a> for one-year terms in 1779 and 1780.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[50]</a></sup> He transferred the state capital from Williamsburg to <a href="/wiki/Richmond,_Virginia" title="Richmond, Virginia">Richmond</a>, and introduced measures for public education, religious freedom, and revision of inheritance laws.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>During General <a href="/wiki/Benedict_Arnold" title="Benedict Arnold">Benedict Arnold</a>'s 1781 invasion of Virginia, Jefferson escaped Richmond just ahead of the British forces, and the city was burned to the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[53]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Cornwallis,_1st_Marquess_Cornwallis" title="Charles Cornwallis, 1st Marquess Cornwallis">General Charles Cornwallis</a> that spring dispatched a cavalry force led by <a href="/wiki/Banastre_Tarleton" title="Banastre Tarleton">Banastre Tarleton</a> to capture Jefferson and members of the Assembly at Monticello, but <a href="/wiki/Jack_Jouett" title="Jack Jouett">Jack Jouett</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_militia" title="Virginia militia">Virginia militia</a> thwarted the British plan. Jefferson escaped to <a href="/wiki/Poplar_Forest" title="Poplar Forest">Poplar Forest</a>, his plantation to the west.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[54]</a></sup> When the General Assembly reconvened in June 1781, it conducted an inquiry into Jefferson's actions which eventually concluded that Jefferson had acted with honor—but he was not re-elected.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>In April of the same year, his daughter Lucy died at age one. A second daughter of that name was born the following year, but she died at age three.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes_on_the_State_of_Virginia"><i>Notes on the State of Virginia</i></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Notes_on_the_State_of_Virginia" title="Notes on the State of Virginia">Notes on the State of Virginia</a></div>
<p>Jefferson received a letter of inquiry in 1780 about the geography, history, and government of Virginia from French diplomat <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Barb%C3%A9-Marbois" title="François Barbé-Marbois">François Barbé-Marbois</a>, who was gathering data on the United States. Jefferson included his written responses in a book, <i>Notes on the State of Virginia</i> (1785).<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[57]</a></sup> He compiled the book over five years, including reviews of scientific knowledge, Virginia's history, politics, laws, culture, and geography.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[58]</a></sup> The book explores what constitutes a good society, using Virginia as an exemplar. Jefferson included extensive data about the state's natural resources and economy, and wrote at length about slavery, <a href="/wiki/Miscegenation" title="Miscegenation">miscegenation</a>, and his belief that blacks and whites could not live together as free people in one society because of justified resentments of the enslaved.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[59]</a></sup> He also wrote of his views on the American Indian and considered them as equals in body and mind to European settlers.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Notes</i> was first published in 1785 in French and appeared in English in 1787.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[62]</a></sup> Biographer <a href="/wiki/George_Tucker_(politician)" title="George Tucker (politician)">George Tucker</a> considered the work "surprising in the extent of the information which a single individual had been thus able to acquire, as to the physical features of the state",<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[63]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Merrill_D._Peterson" title="Merrill D. Peterson">Merrill D. Peterson</a> described it as an accomplishment for which all Americans should be grateful.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Member_of_Congress">Member of Congress</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Independence_Hall_Assembly_Room.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Legislative chamber" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Independence_Hall_Assembly_Room.jpg/220px-Independence_Hall_Assembly_Room.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Independence_Hall_Assembly_Room.jpg/330px-Independence_Hall_Assembly_Room.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Independence_Hall_Assembly_Room.jpg/440px-Independence_Hall_Assembly_Room.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Independence_Hall_Assembly_Room.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Independence Hall Assembly Room where Jefferson served in Congress</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The United States formed a <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_Confederation" title="Congress of the Confederation">Congress of the Confederation</a> following victory in the Revolutionary War and a <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783)" title="Treaty of Paris (1783)">peace treaty</a> with Great Britain in 1783, to which Jefferson was appointed as a Virginia delegate. He was a member of the committee setting foreign exchange rates and <a href="/wiki/Plan_for_Establishing_Uniformity_in_the_Coinage,_Weights,_and_Measures_of_the_United_States" title="Plan for Establishing Uniformity in the Coinage, Weights, and Measures of the United States">recommended</a> an American currency based on the decimal system which was adopted.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[65]</a></sup> He advised formation of the <a href="/wiki/Committee_of_the_States" title="Committee of the States">Committee of the States</a> to fill the power vacuum when Congress was in recess.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[66]</a></sup> The Committee met when Congress adjourned, but disagreements rendered it dysfunctional.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the Congress's 1783–84 session, Jefferson acted as chairman of committees to establish a viable system of government for the new Republic and to propose a policy for the settlement of the western territories. Jefferson was the principal author of the <a href="/wiki/Land_Ordinance_of_1784" title="Land Ordinance of 1784">Land Ordinance of 1784</a>, whereby Virginia ceded to the national government the vast area that it claimed northwest of the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">Ohio River</a>. He insisted that this territory should not be used as colonial territory by any of the thirteen states, but that it should be divided into sections which could become states. He plotted borders for nine new states in their initial stages and wrote an ordinance banning slavery in all the nation's territories. Congress made extensive revisions, including rejection of the ban on slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-Peretson189_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peretson189-72">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[69]</a></sup> The provisions banning slavery were known later as the "Jefferson Proviso;" they were modified and implemented three years later in the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Ordinance" title="Northwest Ordinance">Northwest Ordinance</a> of 1787 and became the law for the entire Northwest.<sup id="cite_ref-Peretson189_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peretson189-72">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Minister_to_France">Minister to France</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mather_Brown_-_Thomas_Jefferson_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Young Thomas Jefferson" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Mather_Brown_-_Thomas_Jefferson_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Mather_Brown_-_Thomas_Jefferson_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="220" height="281" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Mather_Brown_-_Thomas_Jefferson_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Mather_Brown_-_Thomas_Jefferson_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Mather_Brown_-_Thomas_Jefferson_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Mather_Brown_-_Thomas_Jefferson_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7707" data-file-height="9840" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mather_Brown_-_Thomas_Jefferson_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Portrait of Thomas Jefferson while in London in 1786 by <a href="/wiki/Mather_Brown" title="Mather Brown">Mather Brown</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jefferson was sent by the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_Confederation" title="Congress of the Confederation">Congress of the Confederation</a><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[e]</a></sup> to join Benjamin Franklin and John Adams as ministers in Europe for negotiation of trade agreements with England, Spain, and France. Some believed that the recently widowed Jefferson was depressed and that the assignment would distract him from his wife's death.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[70]</a></sup> With his young daughter Patsy and two servants, he departed in July 1784, arriving in Paris the next month.<sup id="cite_ref-Stewart39_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stewart39-76">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[72]</a></sup> French foreign minister <a href="/wiki/Charles_Gravier,_comte_de_Vergennes" title="Charles Gravier, comte de Vergennes">Count de Vergennes</a> commented, "You replace Monsieur Franklin, I hear." Jefferson replied, "I <i>succeed</i>. No man can replace him."<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[73]</a></sup> Franklin resigned as minister in March 1785 and departed in July.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson had Patsy educated at the <a href="/wiki/Pentemont_Abbey" title="Pentemont Abbey">Pentemont Abbey</a>. In 1786, he met and fell in love with <a href="/wiki/Maria_Cosway" title="Maria Cosway">Maria Cosway</a>, an accomplished—and married—Italian-English musician of 27. They saw each other frequently over a period of six weeks. She returned to Great Britain, but they maintained a lifelong correspondence.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson sent for his youngest surviving child, nine-year-old Polly, in June 1787. He also brought some of his slaves with him to Paris, including <a href="/wiki/James_Hemings" title="James Hemings">James Hemings</a>, whom he had trained in French cuisine, and James's younger sister <a href="/wiki/Sally_Hemings" title="Sally Hemings">Sally Hemings</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[76]</a></sup> According to Sally's son, <a href="/wiki/Madison_Hemings" title="Madison Hemings">Madison Hemings</a>, the 16-year-old Sally and Jefferson began a sexual relationship in Paris, where she became pregnant.<sup id="cite_ref-madisonstatement_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-madisonstatement-82">[77]</a></sup> According to this story, Hemings agreed to return to the United States only after Jefferson promised to free her children when they came of age.<sup id="cite_ref-madisonstatement_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-madisonstatement-82">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>While in France, he became a regular companion of the <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_du_Motier,_Marquis_de_Lafayette" title="Gilbert du Motier, Marquis de Lafayette">Marquis de Lafayette</a>, a <a href="/wiki/France_in_the_American_Revolutionary_War" title="France in the American Revolutionary War">French hero of the American Revolutionary War</a>, and Jefferson used his influence to procure trade agreements with France.<sup id="cite_ref-Bowers328_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bowers328-83">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Burstein120_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burstein120-84">[79]</a></sup> As the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> began, Jefferson allowed his Paris residence, the <a href="/wiki/H%C3%B4tel_de_Langeac" title="Hôtel de Langeac">Hôtel de Langeac</a>, to be used for meetings by Lafayette and other republicans. He was in Paris during the storming of the Bastille<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[80]</a></sup> and consulted with Lafayette while the latter drafted the <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_the_Rights_of_Man_and_of_the_Citizen" title="Declaration of the Rights of Man and of the Citizen">Declaration of the Rights of Man and of the Citizen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[81]</a></sup> Jefferson often found his mail opened by postmasters, so he invented his own enciphering device, the "<a href="/wiki/Jefferson_disk" title="Jefferson disk">Wheel Cipher</a>"; he wrote important communications in code for the rest of his career.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[f]</a></sup> Jefferson left Paris in September 1789, intending to return soon; however, President <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> appointed him the country's first Secretary of State, forcing him to remain.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[83]</a></sup> Jefferson remained a firm supporter of the French Revolution, while opposing its more violent elements.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Secretary_of_State">Secretary of State</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/First_Party_System" title="First Party System">First Party System</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Thomas Jefferson" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg/200px-T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg" width="200" height="281" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg/300px-T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/46/T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="506" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Thomas Jefferson in 1791 at 49 by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Willson_Peale" title="Charles Willson Peale">Charles Willson Peale</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Soon after returning from France, Jefferson accepted Washington's invitation to serve as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[85]</a></sup> Jefferson had initially expected to return to France, but Washington insisted that he be on his new <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_States" title="Cabinet of the United States">Cabinet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Randall_1996_p1_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Randall_1996_p1-92">[86]</a></sup> Pressing issues at this time were the national debt and the permanent location of the capital. Jefferson opposed a national debt, preferring that each state retire its own, in contrast to <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Secretary of the Treasury">Secretary of the Treasury</a> <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a>, who desired consolidation of various states' debts by the federal government.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[87]</a></sup> Hamilton also had bold plans to establish the national credit and a national bank, but Jefferson strenuously opposed this and attempted to undermine his agenda, which nearly led Washington to dismiss him from his cabinet. Jefferson later left the cabinet voluntarily; Washington never forgave him, and never spoke to him again.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>The second major issue was the capital's permanent location. Hamilton favored a capital close to the major commercial centers of the Northeast, while Washington, Jefferson, and other agrarians wanted it located to the south.<sup id="cite_ref-Cooke.2C_1970.2C_pp._523.E2.80.9345_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cooke.2C_1970.2C_pp._523.E2.80.9345-95">[89]</a></sup> After lengthy deadlock, the <a href="/wiki/Compromise_of_1790" title="Compromise of 1790">Compromise of 1790</a> was struck, permanently locating the capital on the Potomac River, and the federal government assumed the war debts of all thirteen states.<sup id="cite_ref-Cooke.2C_1970.2C_pp._523.E2.80.9345_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cooke.2C_1970.2C_pp._523.E2.80.9345-95">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the Spring of 1791, Jefferson and Congressman James Madison took a vacation to <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a>. Jefferson had been suffering from migraines and he was tired of Hamilton in-fighting.<sup id="cite_ref-Randall_1996_p1_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Randall_1996_p1-92">[86]</a></sup> In May 1792, Jefferson was alarmed at the political rivalries taking shape; he wrote to Washington, urging him to run for re-election that year as a unifying influence.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[90]</a></sup> He urged the president to rally the citizenry to a party that would defend democracy against the corrupting influence of banks and monied interests, as espoused by the Federalists. Historians recognize this letter as the earliest delineation of <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican Party</a> principles.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[91]</a></sup> Jefferson, Madison, and other Democratic-Republican organizers favored <a href="/wiki/States%27_rights" title="States' rights">states' rights</a> and local control and opposed federal concentration of power, whereas Hamilton sought more power for the federal government.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson supported France against Britain when the two nations fought in 1793, though his arguments in the Cabinet were undercut by French Revolutionary envoy <a href="/wiki/Edmond-Charles_Gen%C3%AAt" title="Edmond-Charles Genêt">Edmond-Charles Genêt</a>'s open scorn for President Washington.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[93]</a></sup> In his discussions with British Minister <a href="/wiki/George_Hammond_(diplomat)" title="George Hammond (diplomat)">George Hammond</a>, Jefferson tried unsuccessfully to persuade the British to acknowledge their violation of the Treaty of Paris, to vacate their posts in the Northwest, and to compensate the U.S. for slaves whom the British had freed at the end of the war. Seeking a return to private life, Jefferson resigned the cabinet position in December 1793, perhaps to bolster his political influence from outside the administration.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the Washington administration negotiated the <a href="/wiki/Jay_Treaty" title="Jay Treaty">Jay Treaty</a> with Great Britain (1794), Jefferson saw a cause around which to rally his party and organized a national opposition from Monticello.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[95]</a></sup> The treaty, designed by Hamilton, aimed to reduce tensions and increase trade. Jefferson warned that it would increase British influence and subvert republicanism, calling it "the boldest act [Hamilton and Jay] ever ventured on to undermine the government".<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[96]</a></sup> The Treaty passed, but it expired in 1805 during Jefferson's administration and was not renewed. Jefferson continued his pro-French stance; during the violence of the <a href="/wiki/Reign_of_Terror" title="Reign of Terror">Reign of Terror</a>, he declined to disavow the revolution: "To back away from France would be to undermine the cause of republicanism in America."<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1796_and_Vice_Presidency">Election of 1796 and Vice Presidency</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1796" title="United States presidential election, 1796">United States presidential election, 1796</a> and <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican Party</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1796.svg" class="image"><img alt="Electoral College map" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/ElectoralCollege1796.svg/220px-ElectoralCollege1796.svg.png" width="220" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/ElectoralCollege1796.svg/330px-ElectoralCollege1796.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/ElectoralCollege1796.svg/440px-ElectoralCollege1796.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="540" data-file-height="443" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1796.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1796 Electoral College vote</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the presidential campaign of 1796, Jefferson lost the electoral college vote to Federalist John Adams by 71–68 and was elected vice president because of a mistake in voting for Adams' running mate. As presiding officer of the Senate, he assumed a more passive role than his predecessor John Adams. He allowed the Senate to freely conduct debates and confined his participation to procedural issues, which he called an "honorable and easy" role.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[98]</a></sup> Jefferson had previously studied parliamentary law and procedure for forty years, making him unusually well qualified to serve as presiding officer. In 1800, he published his assembled notes on Senate procedure as <i><a href="/wiki/Jefferson%27s_Manual" title="Jefferson's Manual">A Manual of Parliamentary Practice</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson held four confidential talks with French consul Joseph Létombe in the spring of 1797 where he attacked Adams, predicting that his rival would serve only one term. He also encouraged France to invade England, and advised Létombe to stall any American envoys sent to Paris by instructing him to "listen to them and then drag out the negotiations at length and mollify them by the urbanity of the proceedings."<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[100]</a></sup> This toughened the tone that the French government adopted toward the Adams administration. After Adams' initial peace envoys were rebuffed, Jefferson and his supporters lobbied for the release of papers related to the incident, called the <a href="/wiki/XYZ_Affair" title="XYZ Affair">XYZ Affair</a> after the letters used to disguise the identities of the French officials involved.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[101]</a></sup> However, the tactic backfired when it was revealed that French officials had demanded bribes, rallying public support against France. The U.S. began an undeclared naval war with France known as the <a href="/wiki/Quasi-War" title="Quasi-War">Quasi-War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the Adams presidency, the Federalists rebuilt the military, levied new taxes, and enacted the <a href="/wiki/Alien_and_Sedition_Acts" title="Alien and Sedition Acts">Alien and Sedition Acts</a>. Jefferson believed that these laws were intended to suppress Democratic-Republicans, rather than prosecute enemy aliens, and considered them unconstitutional.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[103]</a></sup> To rally opposition, he and James Madison anonymously wrote the <a href="/wiki/Kentucky_and_Virginia_Resolutions" title="Kentucky and Virginia Resolutions">Kentucky and Virginia Resolutions</a>, declaring that the federal government had no right to exercise powers not specifically delegated to it by the states.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[104]</a></sup> The resolutions followed the "<a href="/wiki/Interposition" title="Interposition">interposition</a>" approach of Madison, in which states may shield their citizens from federal laws that they deem unconstitutional. Jefferson advocated <a href="/wiki/Nullification_(U.S._Constitution)" title="Nullification (U.S. Constitution)">nullification</a>, allowing states to invalidate federal laws altogether.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[105]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[g]</a></sup> Jefferson warned that, "unless arrested at the threshold", the Alien and Sedition Acts would "necessarily drive these states into revolution and blood".<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historian <a href="/wiki/Ron_Chernow" title="Ron Chernow">Ron Chernow</a> claims that "the theoretical damage of the Kentucky and Virginia Resolutions was deep and lasting, and was a recipe for disunion", contributing to the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> as well as later events.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[108]</a></sup> Washington was so appalled by the resolutions that he told Patrick Henry that, if "systematically and pertinaciously pursued", the resolutions would "dissolve the union or produce coercion."<sup id="cite_ref-Chernow587_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernow587-116">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson and Madison moved to Philadelphia and founded the <a href="/wiki/National_Gazette" title="National Gazette">National Gazette</a> in 1791, along with poet and writer <a href="/wiki/Phillip_Freneau" class="mw-redirect" title="Phillip Freneau">Phillip Freneau</a>, in an effort to counter Hamilton's Federalist policies, which Hamilton was promoting through the influential Federalist newspaper the <a href="/wiki/Gazette_of_the_United_States" title="Gazette of the United States">Gazette of the United States</a>. The National Gazette made particular criticism of the policies promoted by Alexander Hamilton, often through anonymous essays signed by the pen name <i>Brutus</i> at Jefferson's urging, which were actually written by Madison.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson had always admired Washington's leadership skills but felt that his Federalist party was leading the country in the wrong direction. Jefferson thought it wise not to attend his funeral in 1799 because of acute differences with Washington while serving as Secretary of State, and remained at Monticello.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[111]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1800">Election of 1800</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1800.svg" class="image"><img alt="Electoral College map" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/ElectoralCollege1800.svg/220px-ElectoralCollege1800.svg.png" width="220" height="181" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/ElectoralCollege1800.svg/330px-ElectoralCollege1800.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/ElectoralCollege1800.svg/440px-ElectoralCollege1800.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="542" data-file-height="446" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1800.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1800 Electoral College vote</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1800" title="United States presidential election, 1800">United States presidential election, 1800</a></div>
<p>In the 1800 presidential election, Jefferson contended once more against Federalist John Adams. Adams' campaign was weakened by unpopular taxes and vicious Federalist infighting over his actions in the Quasi-War.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[112]</a></sup> Republicans pointed to the Alien and Sedition Acts and accused the Federalists of being secret monarchists, while Federalists charged that Jefferson was a godless libertine in thrall to the French.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[113]</a></sup> Historian Joyce Appleby said the election was "one of the most acrimonious in the annals of American history".<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>Republicans ultimately won more electoral college votes, but Jefferson and his vice presidential candidate <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Burr" title="Aaron Burr">Aaron Burr</a> unexpectedly received an equal total. Due to the tie, the election was decided by the Federalist-dominated House of Representatives.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[115]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[h]</a></sup> Hamilton lobbied Federalist representatives on Jefferson's behalf, believing him a lesser political evil than Burr. On February 17, 1801, after thirty-six ballots, the House elected Jefferson president and Burr vice president.<sup id="cite_ref-W284_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-W284-123">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>The win was marked by Republican celebrations throughout the country.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[117]</a></sup> Some of Jefferson's opponents argued that he owed his victory over Adams to the South's inflated number of electors, due to counting slaves as partial population under the <a href="/wiki/Three-Fifths_Compromise" title="Three-Fifths Compromise">Three-Fifths Compromise</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[118]</a></sup> Others alleged that Jefferson secured <a href="/wiki/James_A._Bayard_(elder)" title="James A. Bayard (elder)">James Asheton Bayard</a>'s tie-breaking electoral vote by guaranteeing the retention of various Federalist posts in the government.<sup id="cite_ref-W284_123-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-W284-123">[116]</a></sup> Jefferson disputed the allegation, and the historical record is inconclusive.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>The transition proceeded smoothly, marking a watershed in American history. As historian <a href="/wiki/Gordon_S._Wood" title="Gordon S. Wood">Gordon S. Wood</a> writes, "it was one of the first popular elections in modern history that resulted in the peaceful transfer of power from one 'party' to another."<sup id="cite_ref-W284_123-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-W284-123">[116]</a></sup></p>

<p>Jefferson was sworn in by <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">Chief Justice</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Marshall" title="John Marshall">John Marshall</a> at the new Capitol in Washington, D.C. on March 4, 1801. In contrast to his predecessors, Jefferson exhibited a dislike of formal etiquette; he arrived alone on horseback without escort, dressed plainly<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[120]</a></sup> and, after dismounting, retired his own horse to the nearby stable.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[121]</a></sup> His inaugural address struck a note of reconciliation, declaring, "We have been called by different names brethren of the same principle. We are all Republicans, we are all Federalists."<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[122]</a></sup> Ideologically, Jefferson stressed "equal and exact justice to all men", minority rights, and freedom of speech, religion, and press.<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson_2002_p40_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson_2002_p40-131">[123]</a></sup> He said that a free and democratic government was "the strongest government on earth."<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson_2002_p40_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson_2002_p40-131">[123]</a></sup> He nominated moderate Republicans to his cabinet: James Madison as Secretary of State, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Dearborn" title="Henry Dearborn">Henry Dearborn</a> as Secretary of War, <a href="/wiki/Levi_Lincoln_Sr." title="Levi Lincoln Sr.">Levi Lincoln</a> as Attorney General, and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Smith_(Cabinet_member)" title="Robert Smith (Cabinet member)">Robert Smith</a> as Secretary of the Navy.<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson41_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson41-132">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p>Upon assuming office, he first confronted an $83 million national debt.<sup id="cite_ref-Meacham387_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham387-133">[125]</a></sup> He began dismantling Hamilton's Federalist fiscal system with help from Secretary of Treasury <a href="/wiki/Albert_Gallatin" title="Albert Gallatin">Albert Gallatin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson41_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson41-132">[124]</a></sup> Jefferson's administration eliminated the whiskey excise and other taxes after closing "unnecessary offices" and cutting "useless establishments and expenses".<sup id="cite_ref-Wood.2C_2010.2C_p._293_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood.2C_2010.2C_p._293-134">[126]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[127]</a></sup> They attempted to disassemble the national bank and its effect of increasing national debt, but were dissuaded by Gallatin.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[128]</a></sup> Jefferson shrank the Navy, deeming it unnecessary in peacetime.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[129]</a></sup> Instead, he incorporated a fleet of inexpensive gunboats used only for defense with the idea that they would not provoke foreign hostilities.<sup id="cite_ref-Wood.2C_2010.2C_p._293_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood.2C_2010.2C_p._293-134">[126]</a></sup> After two terms, he had lowered the national debt from $83 million to $57 million.<sup id="cite_ref-Meacham387_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham387-133">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson pardoned several of those imprisoned under the Alien and Sedition Acts.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[130]</a></sup> Congressional Republicans repealed the <a href="/wiki/Midnight_Judges_Act" title="Midnight Judges Act">Judiciary Act of 1801</a>, which removed nearly all of Adams' "midnight judges" from office. A subsequent appointment battle led to the Supreme Court's landmark decision in <i><a href="/wiki/Marbury_v._Madison" title="Marbury v. Madison">Marbury v. Madison</a></i>, asserting judicial review over executive branch actions.<sup id="cite_ref-Meacham.2C_2012.2C_p._413_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham.2C_2012.2C_p._413-139">[131]</a></sup> Jefferson appointed three <a href="/wiki/Associate_Justice_of_the_Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Associate Justice of the Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court justices</a>: <a href="/wiki/William_Johnson_(judge)" title="William Johnson (judge)">William Johnson</a> (1804), <a href="/wiki/Henry_Brockholst_Livingston" title="Henry Brockholst Livingston">Henry Brockholst Livingston</a> (1807), and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Todd" title="Thomas Todd">Thomas Todd</a> (1807).<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson strongly felt the need for a national military university, producing an officer engineering corps for a national defense based on the advancement of the sciences, rather than having to rely on foreign sources for top grade engineers with questionable loyalty.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[133]</a></sup> He signed the <a href="/wiki/Military_Peace_Establishment_Act" title="Military Peace Establishment Act">Military Peace Establishment Act</a> on March 16, 1802, thus founding the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Military_Academy" title="United States Military Academy">United States Military Academy</a> at West Point. The Act documented in 29 sections a new set of laws and limits for the military. Jefferson was also hoping to bring reform to the Executive branch, replacing Federalists and active opponents throughout the officer corps to promote Republican values.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[134]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_Barbary_War">First Barbary War</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/First_Barbary_War" title="First Barbary War">First Barbary War</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:366px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1800_map_Afrique_by_Arrowsmith_BPL_15210_detail2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Map. Barbary Coast of North Africa 1806" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/1800_map_Afrique_by_Arrowsmith_BPL_15210_detail2.jpg/364px-1800_map_Afrique_by_Arrowsmith_BPL_15210_detail2.jpg" width="364" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/1800_map_Afrique_by_Arrowsmith_BPL_15210_detail2.jpg/546px-1800_map_Afrique_by_Arrowsmith_BPL_15210_detail2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/1800_map_Afrique_by_Arrowsmith_BPL_15210_detail2.jpg/728px-1800_map_Afrique_by_Arrowsmith_BPL_15210_detail2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1247" data-file-height="498" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1800_map_Afrique_by_Arrowsmith_BPL_15210_detail2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Barbary Coast of North Africa 1806. Left is Morocco at Gibraltar, center is Tunis, and right is Tripoli.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>American merchant ships had been protected from <a href="/wiki/Barbary_Coast" title="Barbary Coast">Barbary Coast</a> pirates by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> when the states were British colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[135]</a></sup> After independence, however, pirates often captured U.S. merchant ships, pillaged cargoes, and enslaved or held crew members for ransom. Jefferson had opposed paying tribute to the Barbary States since 1785.<sup id="cite_ref-Barnes36_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes36-144">[136]</a></sup> In March 1786, he and John Adams went to London to negotiate with Tripoli's envoy, ambassador Sidi Haji Abdrahaman (or Sidi Haji Abdul Rahman Adja).<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[137]</a></sup> In 1801, he authorized a U.S. Navy fleet under Commodore <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dale" title="Richard Dale">Richard Dale</a> to make a show of force in the Mediterranean, the first American naval squadron to cross the Atlantic.<sup id="cite_ref-Meacham_pp._364_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham_pp._364-146">[138]</a></sup> Following the fleet's first engagement, he successfully asked Congress for a declaration of war.<sup id="cite_ref-Meacham_pp._364_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham_pp._364-146">[138]</a></sup> The subsequent "First Barbary War" was the first foreign war fought by the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>Pasha of <a href="/wiki/Tripoli" title="Tripoli">Tripoli</a> <a href="/wiki/Yusuf_Karamanli" title="Yusuf Karamanli">Yusuf Karamanli</a> captured the <a href="/wiki/USS_Philadelphia_(1799)" title="USS Philadelphia (1799)">USS <i>Philadelphia</i></a>, so Jefferson authorized <a href="/wiki/William_Eaton_(soldier)" title="William Eaton (soldier)">William Eaton</a>, the U.S. Consul to <a href="/wiki/Tunis" title="Tunis">Tunis</a>, to lead a force to restore the pasha's older brother to the throne.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[140]</a></sup> The American navy forced Tunis and <a href="/wiki/Algiers" title="Algiers">Algiers</a> into breaking their alliance with Tripoli. Jefferson ordered five separate naval bombardments of Tripoli, leading the pasha to sign a treaty that restored peace in the Mediterranean.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[141]</a></sup> This victory proved only temporary, but according to Wood, "many Americans celebrated it as a vindication of their policy of spreading free trade around the world and as a great victory for liberty over tyranny."<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Louisiana_Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:366px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Louisiana_Purchase.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Louisiana_Purchase.png/364px-Louisiana_Purchase.png" width="364" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Louisiana_Purchase.png/546px-Louisiana_Purchase.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Louisiana_Purchase.png/728px-Louisiana_Purchase.png 2x" data-file-width="3507" data-file-height="2480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Louisiana_Purchase.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The 1803 Louisiana Purchase totaled 827,987 <a href="/wiki/Square_mile" title="Square mile">square miles</a> (2,144,480 square kilometres), doubling the size of the United States</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Spain <a href="/wiki/Third_Treaty_of_San_Ildefonso" title="Third Treaty of San Ildefonso">ceded ownership</a> of the Louisiana territory in 1800 to the more predominant France. Jefferson was greatly concerned that <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a>'s broad interests in the vast territory would threaten the security of the continent and <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a> shipping. He wrote that the cession "works most sorely on the U.S. It completely reverses all the political relations of the U.S." <sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[143]</a></sup> In 1802, he instructed <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe" title="James Monroe">James Monroe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_R._Livingston_(chancellor)" title="Robert R. Livingston (chancellor)">Robert R. Livingston</a> to negotiate with Napoleon to purchase <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a> and adjacent coastal areas from France.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[144]</a></sup> In early 1803, Jefferson offered Napoleon nearly $10 million for 40,000 square miles (100,000 square kilometres) of tropical territory.<sup id="cite_ref-Freehling2005_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Freehling2005-153">[145]</a></sup></p>
<p>Napoleon realized that French military control was impractical over such a vast remote territory, and he was in dire need of funds for his <a href="/wiki/The_United_Kingdom_in_the_Napoleonic_Wars" title="The United Kingdom in the Napoleonic Wars">wars on the home front</a>.
