In early April 1803, he unexpectedly made negotiators a counter-offer to sell 827,987 square miles (2,144,480 square kilometres) of French territory for $15 million, doubling the size of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Freehling2005_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Freehling2005-153">[145]</a></sup> U.S. negotiators seized this unique opportunity and accepted the offer and signed the treaty on April 30, 1803.<sup id="cite_ref-Meacham387_133-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham387-133">[125]</a></sup> Word of the unexpected purchase didn't reach Jefferson until July 3, 1803.<sup id="cite_ref-Meacham387_133-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham387-133">[125]</a></sup> He unknowingly acquired the most fertile tract of land of its size on Earth, making the new country self-sufficient in food and other resources. The sale also significantly curtailed British and French imperial ambitions in North America, removing obstacles to U.S. westward expansion.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis208_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis208-154">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most thought that this was an exceptional opportunity, despite Republican reservations about the Constitutional authority of the federal government to acquire land.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Rise_of_American_Democracy_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Rise_of_American_Democracy-155">[147]</a></sup> Jefferson initially thought that a Constitutional <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States_Constitution#Senate_changes" title="History of the United States Constitution">amendment</a> was necessary to purchase and govern the new territory; but he later changed his mind, fearing that this would give cause to oppose the purchase, and he therefore urged a speedy debate and ratification.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[148]</a></sup> On October 20, 1803, the Senate ratified the purchase treaty by a vote of 24–7.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[149]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the purchase, Jefferson preserved the region's Spanish legal code and instituted a gradual approach for integrating settlers into American democracy. He believed that a period of federal rule would be necessary while Louisianians adjusted to their new nation.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[150]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[i]</a></sup> Historians have differed in their assessments regarding the constitutional implications of the sale,<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[152]</a></sup> but they typically hail the Louisiana acquisition as a major accomplishment. <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Jackson_Turner" title="Frederick Jackson Turner">Frederick Jackson Turner</a> called the purchase the most formative event in American history.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis208_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis208-154">[146]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lewis_and_Clark_expedition">Lewis and Clark expedition</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Lewis_and_Clark_Expedition" title="Lewis and Clark Expedition">Lewis and Clark Expedition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Red_River_Expedition_(1806)" title="Red River Expedition (1806)">Red River Expedition (1806)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pike_expedition" title="Pike expedition">Pike expedition</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lewis_and_clark-expedition.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Corps of Discover on river boat October 1805" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Lewis_and_clark-expedition.jpg/200px-Lewis_and_clark-expedition.jpg" width="200" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Lewis_and_clark-expedition.jpg/300px-Lewis_and_clark-expedition.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Lewis_and_clark-expedition.jpg/400px-Lewis_and_clark-expedition.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2346" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lewis_and_clark-expedition.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Corps of Discovery<br />
October 1805</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jefferson anticipated further westward settlements due to the Louisiana Purchase and arranged for the exploration and mapping of the uncharted territory. He sought to establish a U.S. claim ahead of competing European interests and to find the rumored <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Passage" title="Northwest Passage">Northwest Passage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ambrose76_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ambrose76-162">[153]</a></sup> Jefferson and others were influenced by exploration accounts of <a href="/wiki/Antoine-Simon_Le_Page_du_Pratz" title="Antoine-Simon Le Page du Pratz">Le Page du Pratz</a> in Louisiana (1763) and Captain <a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">James Cook</a> in the Pacific (1784),<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[154]</a></sup> and they persuaded Congress in 1804 to fund an expedition to explore and <a href="/wiki/File:Lewis_and_Clark_Expidition_Map.jpg" title="File:Lewis and Clark Expidition Map.jpg">map</a> the newly acquired territory to the Pacific Ocean.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[155]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson appointed <a href="/wiki/Meriwether_Lewis" title="Meriwether Lewis">Meriwether Lewis</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Clark" title="William Clark">William Clark</a> to be leaders of the <a href="/wiki/Corps_of_Discovery" title="Corps of Discovery">Corps of Discovery</a> (1803–1806).<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[156]</a></sup> In the months leading up to the expedition, Jefferson tutored Lewis in the sciences of mapping, botany, natural history, mineralogy, and astronomy and navigation, giving him unlimited access to his library at Monticello, which included the largest collection of books in the world on the subject of the geography and natural history of the North American continent, along with an impressive collection of maps.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>The expedition lasted from May 1804 to September 1806 <a href="/wiki/Template:Timeline_of_the_Lewis_and_Clark_Expedition" title="Template:Timeline of the Lewis and Clark Expedition">(see Timeline)</a> and obtained a wealth of scientific and geographic knowledge, including knowledge of many Indian tribes.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[158]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt><big>Other expeditions</big></dt>
</dl>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Red_River_Expedition_(1806)" title="Red River Expedition (1806)">Red River Expedition (1806)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pike_expedition" title="Pike expedition">Pike expedition</a></div>
<p>In addition to the Corps of Discovery, Jefferson organized three other western expeditions: the <a href="/wiki/William_Dunbar_(explorer)" title="William Dunbar (explorer)">William Dunbar</a> and George Hunter expedition on the <a href="/wiki/Ouachita_River" title="Ouachita River">Ouachita River</a> (1804–1805), the <a href="/wiki/Red_River_Expedition_(1806)" title="Red River Expedition (1806)">Thomas Freeman and Peter Custis expedition</a> (1806) on the <a href="/wiki/Red_River_of_the_South" title="Red River of the South">Red River</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Pike_expedition" title="Pike expedition">Zebulon Pike expedition</a> (1806–1807) into the Rocky Mountains and the Southwest. All three produced valuable information about the American frontier.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[159]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="American_Indian_policies">American Indian policies</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson_and_Native_Americans" title="Thomas Jefferson and Native Americans">Thomas Jefferson and Native Americans</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Black_Hoof.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Black_Hoof.jpg/180px-Black_Hoof.jpg" width="180" height="203" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Black_Hoof.jpg/270px-Black_Hoof.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Black_Hoof.jpg/360px-Black_Hoof.jpg 2x" data-file-width="412" data-file-height="464" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Black_Hoof.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Black Hoof, leader of the Shawnee, accepted Jefferson's Indian assimilation policies.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jefferson's experiences with the American Indians began during his boyhood in Virginia and extended through his political career and into his retirement. He refuted the contemporary notion that Indians were an inferior people and maintained that they were equal in body and mind to people of European descent.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p>As governor of Virginia during the Revolutionary War, Jefferson recommended moving the <a href="/wiki/Cherokee" title="Cherokee">Cherokee</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shawnee" title="Shawnee">Shawnee</a> tribes, who had allied with the British, to west of the Mississippi River. But when he took office as President, he quickly took measures to avert another major conflict, as American and Indian societies were in collision and the British were inciting Indian tribes from Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-Miller2008_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller2008-170">[161]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sheehan1974_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sheehan1974-171">[162]</a></sup> In Georgia, he stipulated that the state would release its legal claims for lands to its west in exchange for military support in expelling the Cherokee from Georgia. This facilitated his policy of western expansion, to "advance compactly as we multiply".<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[163]</a></sup></p>
<p>In keeping with his <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> thinking, President Jefferson adopted an assimilation policy towards American Indians known as his "civilization program" which included securing peaceful U.S. <b>–</b> Indian treaty alliances and encouraging agriculture. Jefferson advocated that Indian tribes should make federal purchases by credit holding their lands as collateral for repayment. Various tribes accepted Jefferson's policies, including the Shawnees led by <a href="/wiki/Black_Hoof" title="Black Hoof">Black Hoof</a>, the Creek, and the Cherokees. However, some Shawnees broke off from Black Hoof, led by <a href="/wiki/Tecumseh" title="Tecumseh">Tecumseh</a>, and opposed Jefferson's assimilation policies.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[164]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historian Bernard Sheehan argues that Jefferson believed that assimilation was best for American Indians; second best was removal to the west. He felt that the worst outcome of the cultural and resources conflict between American citizens and American Indians would be their attacking the whites.<sup id="cite_ref-Sheehan1974_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sheehan1974-171">[162]</a></sup> Jefferson told Secretary of War General <a href="/wiki/Henry_Dearborn" title="Henry Dearborn">Henry Dearborn</a> (Indian affairs were then under the War Department), "If we are constrained to lift the hatchet against any tribe, we will never lay it down until that tribe is exterminated or driven beyond the Mississippi." <sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[165]</a></sup> Miller agrees that Jefferson believed that Indians should assimilate to American customs and agriculture. Historians such as <a href="/wiki/Peter_S._Onuf" title="Peter S. Onuf">Peter S. Onuf</a> and Merrill D. Peterson argue that Jefferson's actual Indian policies did little to promote assimilation and were a pretext to seize lands.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[166]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Re-election_in_1804_and_second_term">Re-election in 1804 and second term</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1804" title="United States presidential election, 1804">United States presidential election, 1804</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1804.svg" class="image"><img alt="Electoral College map" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/ElectoralCollege1804.svg/220px-ElectoralCollege1804.svg.png" width="220" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/ElectoralCollege1804.svg/330px-ElectoralCollege1804.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/ElectoralCollege1804.svg/440px-ElectoralCollege1804.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="577" data-file-height="593" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1804.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1804 Electoral College vote</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jefferson's successful first term occasioned his re-nomination for president by the Republican party, with <a href="/wiki/George_Clinton_(vice_president)" title="George Clinton (vice president)">George Clinton</a> replacing Burr as his running mate.<sup id="cite_ref-Meacham405_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham405-176">[167]</a></sup> The Federalist party ran <a href="/wiki/Charles_Cotesworth_Pinckney" title="Charles Cotesworth Pinckney">Charles Cotesworth Pinckney</a> of South Carolina, John Adams' vice presidential candidate in the 1800 election. The Jefferson-Clinton ticket won overwhelmingly in the electoral college vote, by 162 to 14, promoting their achievement of a strong economy, lower taxes, and the Louisiana Purchase.<sup id="cite_ref-Meacham405_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham405-176">[167]</a></sup></p>
<p>In March 1806, a split developed in the Republican party, led by fellow Virginian and former Republican ally <a href="/wiki/John_Randolph_of_Roanoke" title="John Randolph of Roanoke">John Randolph</a> who viciously accused President Jefferson on the floor of the House of moving too far in the Federalist direction. In so doing, Randolph permanently set himself apart <a href="/wiki/Tertium_quids" title="Tertium quids">politically</a> from Jefferson. Jefferson and Madison had backed resolutions to limit or ban British imports in retaliation for British actions against American shipping. Also, in 1808, Jefferson was the first president to propose a broad Federal plan to build roads and canals across several states, asking for $20 million, further alarming Randolph and believers of limited government.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[168]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson's popularity further suffered in his second term due to his response to wars in Europe. Positive relations with Great Britain had diminished, due partly to the antipathy between Jefferson and British diplomat <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Merry" title="Anthony Merry">Anthony Merry</a>. After Napoleon's decisive victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Austerlitz" title="Battle of Austerlitz">Battle of Austerlitz</a> in 1805, Napoleon became more aggressive in his negotiations over trading rights, which American efforts failed to counter. Jefferson then led the enactment of the <a href="/wiki/Embargo_Act_of_1807" title="Embargo Act of 1807">Embargo Act of 1807</a>, directed at both France and Great Britain. This triggered economic chaos in the U.S. and was strongly criticized at the time, resulting in Jefferson having to abandon the policy a year later.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[169]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the revolutionary era, the states abolished the international slave trade, but South Carolina reopened it. In his annual message of December 1806, Jefferson denounced the "violations of human rights" attending the international slave trade, calling on the newly elected Congress to criminalize it immediately. In 1807, Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/Act_Prohibiting_Importation_of_Slaves" title="Act Prohibiting Importation of Slaves">Act Prohibiting Importation of Slaves</a>, which Jefferson signed.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[170]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Randal583_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Randal583-180">[171]</a></sup> The act established severe punishment against the international slave trade, although it did not address the issue domestically.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[172]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the wake of the Louisiana Purchase, Jefferson sought to annex Florida from Spain, as brokered by Napoleon.<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson2002p49_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson2002p49-182">[173]</a></sup> Congress agreed to the President's request to secretly appropriate purchase money in the "$2,000,000 Bill".<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson2002p49_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson2002p49-182">[173]</a></sup> The Congressional funding drew criticism from Randolph, who believed that the money would wind up in the coffers of Napoleon. The bill was signed into law; however, negotiations for the project failed. Jefferson lost clout among fellow Republicans, and his use of unofficial Congressional channels was sharply criticized.<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson2002p49_182-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson2002p49-182">[173]</a></sup> In Haiti, Jefferson's neutrality had allowed arms to enable the slave independence movement during its <a href="/wiki/Haitian_Revolution" title="Haitian Revolution">Revolution</a>, and blocked attempts to assist Napoleon, who was defeated there in 1803.<sup id="cite_ref-jstorhaiti_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jstorhaiti-183">[174]</a></sup> But he refused official recognition of the country during his second term, in deference to southern complaints about the racial violence against slave-holders; it was eventually extended to Haiti in 1862.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[175]</a></sup> Domestically, Jefferson's grandson James Madison Randolph became the first child born in the White House in 1806.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[176]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Burr_conspiracy_and_trial">Burr conspiracy and trial</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Burr%E2%80%93Hamilton_duel" title="Burr–Hamilton duel">Burr–Hamilton duel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burr_conspiracy" title="Burr conspiracy">Burr conspiracy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Vanderlyn_Burr.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Vanderlyn_Burr.jpg/175px-Vanderlyn_Burr.jpg" width="175" height="238" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Vanderlyn_Burr.jpg/263px-Vanderlyn_Burr.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Vanderlyn_Burr.jpg/350px-Vanderlyn_Burr.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2206" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Vanderlyn_Burr.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Aaron Burr<br />
Vanderlyn <i>1802</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following the 1801 electoral deadlock, Jefferson's relationship rapidly eroded with his Vice President, former New York Senator <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Burr" title="Aaron Burr">Aaron Burr</a>. Jefferson suspected Burr of seeking the presidency for himself, while Burr was angered by Jefferson's refusal to appoint some of his supporters to federal office. Burr was dropped from the Republican ticket in 1804.</p>
<p>The same year, Burr was soundly defeated in his bid to be elected New York governor. During the campaign, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a> publicly made callous remarks regarding Burr's moral character.<sup id="cite_ref-Chernow04_p714_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernow04_p714-186">[177]</a></sup> Subsequently, Burr challenged Hamilton to a duel, mortally wounding and killing Hamilton on July 11, 1804. Burr was indicted for Hamilton's murder in New York and New Jersey, causing him to flee to Georgia, although he remained President of the Senate during Supreme Court Justice <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Chase" title="Samuel Chase">Samuel Chase</a>'s impeachment trial.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[178]</a></sup> Both indictments quietly died and Burr was not prosecuted.<sup id="cite_ref-Banner_1974_p34_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Banner_1974_p34-188">[179]</a></sup> Also during the election, certain New England separatists approached Burr, desiring a New England federation and intimating that he would be their leader.<sup id="cite_ref-Banner_1974_pp34-35_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Banner_1974_pp34-35-189">[180]</a></sup> However, nothing came of the plot, since Burr had lost the election and his reputation was ruined after killing Hamilton.<sup id="cite_ref-Banner_1974_pp34-35_189-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Banner_1974_pp34-35-189">[180]</a></sup> In August 1804, Burr contacted British Minister <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Merry" title="Anthony Merry">Anthony Merry</a> offering to capture U.S. western territory in return for money and British ships.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Burr_Conspiracy_2000_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Burr_Conspiracy_2000-190">[181]</a></sup></p>
<p>After leaving office in April 1805, Burr traveled west and conspired with Louisiana Territory governor <a href="/wiki/James_Wilkinson" title="James Wilkinson">James Wilkinson</a>, beginning a large-scale recruitment for a military expedition.<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson_2002_p50_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson_2002_p50-191">[182]</a></sup> Other plotters included Ohio Senator <a href="/wiki/John_Smith_(Ohio_Senator)" title="John Smith (Ohio Senator)">John Smith</a> and an Irishman named <a href="/wiki/Harman_Blennerhassett" title="Harman Blennerhassett">Harmon Blennerhassett</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson_2002_p50_191-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson_2002_p50-191">[182]</a></sup> Burr discussed a number of plots—seizing control of Mexico or Spanish Florida, or forming a secessionist state in New Orleans or the Western U.S. Historians remain unclear as to his true goal.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[183]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[j]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the fall of 1806, Burr launched a military flotilla carrying about 60 men down the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">Ohio River</a>. Wilkinson renounced the plot, apparently from self-interested motives; he reported Burr's expedition to Jefferson, who immediately ordered Burr's arrest.<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson_2002_p50_191-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson_2002_p50-191">[182]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[185]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Banner_1974_p37_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Banner_1974_p37-196">[186]</a></sup> On February 13, 1807, Burr was captured in Louisiana's Bayou Pierre wilderness and sent to Virginia to be tried for treason.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Burr_Conspiracy_2000_190-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Burr_Conspiracy_2000-190">[181]</a></sup></p>
<p>Burr's 1807 conspiracy trial became a national issue.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[187]</a></sup> Jefferson attempted to preemptively influence the verdict by telling Congress that Burr's guilt was "beyond question", but the case came before his longtime political foe <a href="/wiki/John_Marshall" title="John Marshall">John Marshall</a>, who dismissed the treason charge. Burr's legal team at one stage subpoenaed Jefferson, but Jefferson refused to testify, making the first argument for <a href="/wiki/Executive_privilege" title="Executive privilege">executive privilege</a>. Instead, Jefferson provided relevant legal documents.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[188]</a></sup> After a three-month trial, the jury found Burr not guilty, while Jefferson denounced his acquittal.<sup id="cite_ref-Banner37_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Banner37-199">[189]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[190]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[k]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[191]</a></sup> Jefferson subsequently removed Wilkinson as territorial governor but retained him in the U.S. military. Historian James N. Banner criticized Jefferson for continuing to trust Wilkinson, a "faithless plotter".<sup id="cite_ref-Banner_1974_p37_196-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Banner_1974_p37-196">[186]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chesapeake.E2.80.93Leopard_Affair_and_Embargo_Act">Chesapeake–Leopard Affair and Embargo Act</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chesapeake%E2%80%93Leopard_Affair" title="Chesapeake–Leopard Affair">Chesapeake–Leopard Affair</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Leopardchesapeake.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Leopardchesapeake.jpg/300px-Leopardchesapeake.jpg" width="300" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Leopardchesapeake.jpg/450px-Leopardchesapeake.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Leopardchesapeake.jpg/600px-Leopardchesapeake.jpg 2x" data-file-width="738" data-file-height="378" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Leopardchesapeake.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
HMS <i>Leopard</i> (right) firing upon USS <i>Chesapeake</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The British conducted raids on American shipping and kidnapped seamen in 1806–07; thousands of Americans were thus <a href="/wiki/Impressment" title="Impressment">impressed</a> into the British naval service. In 1806, Jefferson issued a call for a boycott of British goods; on April 18, Congress passed the Non-Importation Acts, but they were never enforced. Later that year, Jefferson asked James Monroe and <a href="/wiki/William_Pinkney" title="William Pinkney">William Pinkney</a> to negotiate with Great Britain to end the harassment of American shipping, though Britain showed no signs of improving relations. The <a href="/wiki/Monroe%E2%80%93Pinkney_Treaty" title="Monroe–Pinkney Treaty">Monroe–Pinkney Treaty</a> was finalized but lacked any provisions to end impressment, and Jefferson refused to submit it to the Senate for ratification.<sup id="cite_ref-Hayes505_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hayes505-203">[192]</a></sup></p>
<p>The British ship <a href="/wiki/HMS_Leopard_(1790)" title="HMS Leopard (1790)">HMS <i>Leopard</i></a> fired upon the <a href="/wiki/USS_Chesapeake_(1799)" title="USS Chesapeake (1799)">USS <i>Chesapeake</i></a> off the Virginia coast in June 1807, and Jefferson prepared for war.<sup id="cite_ref-embargo_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-embargo-204">[193]</a></sup> He issued a proclamation banning armed British ships from U.S. waters. He presumed unilateral authority to call on the states to prepare 100,000 militia and ordered the purchase of arms, ammunition, and supplies, writing, "The laws of necessity, of self-preservation, of saving our country when in danger, are of higher obligation [than strict observance of written laws]". The <a href="/wiki/USS_Revenge_(1806)" title="USS Revenge (1806)">USS <i>Revenge</i></a> was dispatched to demand an explanation from the British government; it also was fired upon. Jefferson called for a special session of Congress in October to enact an embargo or alternatively to consider war.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[194]</a></sup></p>
<p>In December, news arrived that Napoleon had extended the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Decree" title="Berlin Decree">Berlin Decree</a>, globally banning British imports. In Britain, <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">King George III</a> ordered redoubling efforts at impressment, including American sailors. But the war fever of the summer faded; Congress had no appetite to prepare the U.S. for war. Jefferson asked for and received the Embargo Act, an alternative that allowed the U.S. more time to build up defensive works, militias, and naval forces. Later historians have seen irony in Jefferson's assertion of such federal power. Meacham claims that the Embargo Act was a projection of power which surpassed the Alien and Sedition Acts, and R.B. Bernstein writes that Jefferson "was pursuing policies resembling those he had cited in 1776 as grounds for independence and revolution".<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[195]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ograbme.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A turtle biting a man carrying a barrel to a waiting ship" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Ograbme.jpg/220px-Ograbme.jpg" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Ograbme.jpg/330px-Ograbme.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Ograbme.jpg/440px-Ograbme.jpg 2x" data-file-width="576" data-file-height="440" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ograbme.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A political cartoon showing merchants dodging the "Ograbme", which is "Embargo" spelled backwards (1807).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Secretary of State James Madison supported the embargo with equal vigor to Jefferson,<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[196]</a></sup> while Treasury Secretary Gallatin opposed it, due to its indefinite time frame and the risk that it posed to the policy of American neutrality.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[197]</a></sup> The U.S. economy suffered, criticism grew, and opponents began evading the embargo. Instead of retreating, Jefferson sent federal agents to secretly track down smugglers and violators.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[198]</a></sup> Three acts were passed in Congress during 1807 and 1808, called the <i>Supplementary</i>, the <i>Additional</i>, and the <i>Enforcement</i> acts.<sup id="cite_ref-embargo_204-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-embargo-204">[193]</a></sup> The government could not prevent American vessels from trading with the European belligerents once they had left American ports, although the embargo triggered a devastating decline in exports.<sup id="cite_ref-embargo_204-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-embargo-204">[193]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most historians consider Jefferson's embargo to have been ineffective and harmful to American interests.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[199]</a></sup> Appleby describes the strategy as Jefferson's "least effective policy", and Joseph Ellis calls it "an unadulterated calamity".<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[200]</a></sup> Others, however, portray it as an innovative, nonviolent measure which aided France in its war with Britain while preserving American neutrality.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[201]</a></sup> Jefferson believed that the failure of the embargo was due to selfish traders and merchants showing a lack of "republican virtue." He maintained that, had the embargo been widely observed, it would have avoided war in 1812.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[202]</a></sup></p>
<p>In December 1807, Jefferson announced his intention not to seek a third term. He turned his attention increasingly to Monticello during the last year of his presidency, giving Madison and Gallatin almost total control of affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[203]</a></sup> Shortly before leaving office in March 1809, Jefferson signed the repeal of the Embargo. In its place, the <a href="/wiki/Non-Intercourse_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-Intercourse Act">Non-Intercourse Act</a> was passed, but it proved no more effective.<sup id="cite_ref-embargo_204-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-embargo-204">[193]</a></sup> The day before Madison was inaugurated as his successor, Jefferson said that he felt like "a prisoner, released from his chains".<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[204]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Retirement_and_later_years">Retirement and later years</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson_and_education" title="Thomas Jefferson and education">Thomas Jefferson and education</a></div>
<p>Following his retirement from the presidency, Jefferson continued his pursuit of educational interests; he sold his vast collection of books to the Library of Congress, and founded and built the University of Virginia.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[205]</a></sup> Jefferson continued to correspond with many of the country's leaders, and the <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Doctrine" title="Monroe Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</a> bears a strong resemblance to solicited advice that Jefferson gave to Monroe in 1823.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[206]</a></sup> As he settled into private life at Monticello, Jefferson developed a daily routine of rising early. He would spend several hours writing letters, with which he was often deluged. In the midday, he would often inspect the plantation on horseback. In the evenings, his family enjoyed leisure time in the gardens; late at night, Jefferson would retire to bed with a book.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[207]</a></sup> However, his routine was often interrupted by uninvited visitors and tourists eager to see the icon in his final days, turning Monticello into "a virtual hotel".<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[208]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="University_of_Virginia">University of Virginia</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia" title="University of Virginia">University of Virginia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lawn_UVa_colorful_winter_sun_2010.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Lawn_UVa_colorful_winter_sun_2010.jpg/300px-Lawn_UVa_colorful_winter_sun_2010.jpg" width="300" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Lawn_UVa_colorful_winter_sun_2010.jpg/450px-Lawn_UVa_colorful_winter_sun_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Lawn_UVa_colorful_winter_sun_2010.jpg/600px-Lawn_UVa_colorful_winter_sun_2010.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3024" data-file-height="2016" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lawn_UVa_colorful_winter_sun_2010.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The University of Virginia, Jefferson's "Academical Village"</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jefferson envisioned a university free of church influences where students could specialize in many new areas not offered at other colleges. He believed that education engendered a stable society, which should provide publicly funded schools accessible to students from all social strata, based solely on ability.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[209]</a></sup> He initially proposed his University in a letter to <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley" title="Joseph Priestley">Joseph Priestley</a> in 1800<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[210]</a></sup> and, in 1819, the 76-year-old Jefferson founded the University of Virginia. He organized the state legislative campaign for its charter and, with the assistance of <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Bacon_(1785%E2%80%931866)" title="Edmund Bacon (1785–1866)">Edmund Bacon</a>, purchased the location. He was the principal designer of the buildings, planned the university's curriculum, and served as the first rector upon its opening in 1825.<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson_1970_ch11_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson_1970_ch11-222">[211]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson was a strong disciple of Greek and Roman architectural styles, which he believed to be most representative of American democracy. Each academic unit, called a pavilion, was designed with a two-story temple front, while the library "Rotunda" was modeled on the <a href="/wiki/Pantheon,_Rome" title="Pantheon, Rome">Roman Pantheon</a>. Jefferson referred to the university's grounds as the "<a href="/wiki/The_Lawn" title="The Lawn">Academical Village</a>," and he reflected his educational ideas in its layout. The ten pavilions included classrooms and faculty residences; they formed a quadrangle and were connected by colonnades, behind which stood the students' rows of rooms. Gardens and vegetable plots were placed behind the pavilions and were surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Crinkle_crankle_wall" title="Crinkle crankle wall">serpentine walls</a>, affirming the importance of the agrarian lifestyle.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[212]</a></sup> The university had a library rather than a church at its center, emphasizing its secular nature—a controversial aspect at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[213]</a></sup></p>
<p>When Jefferson died in 1826, James Madison replaced him as rector.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">[214]</a></sup> Jefferson bequeathed most of his library to the university.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[215]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reconciliation_with_Adams">Reconciliation with Adams</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Abigail_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Abigail_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart.jpg/150px-Abigail_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart.jpg" width="150" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Abigail_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart.jpg/225px-Abigail_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Abigail_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart.jpg/300px-Abigail_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="4000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Abigail_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In 1804, Abigail Adams attempted to reconcile Jefferson and Adams.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jefferson and John Adams had been good friends in the first decades of their political careers, serving together in the Continental Congress in the 1770s and in Europe in the 1780s. The Federalist/Republican split of the 1790s divided them, however, and Adams felt betrayed by Jefferson's sponsorship of partisan attacks, such as those of James Callender. Jefferson, on the other hand, was angered at Adams for his appointment of "midnight judges".<sup id="cite_ref-Freeman2008_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Freeman2008-227">[216]</a></sup> The two men did not communicate directly for more than a decade after Jefferson succeeded Adams as president.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">[217]</a></sup> A brief correspondence took place between <a href="/wiki/Abigail_Adams" title="Abigail Adams">Abigail Adams</a> and Jefferson after Jefferson's daughter "Polly" died in 1804, in an attempt at reconciliation unknown to Adams. However, an exchange of letters resumed open hostilities between Adams and Jefferson.<sup id="cite_ref-Freeman2008_227-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Freeman2008-227">[216]</a></sup></p>
<p>As early as 1809, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Rush" title="Benjamin Rush">Benjamin Rush</a>, signer of the Declaration of Independence, desired that Jefferson and Adams reconcile and began to prod the two through correspondence to re-establish contact.<sup id="cite_ref-Freeman2008_227-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Freeman2008-227">[216]</a></sup> In 1812, Adams wrote a short New Year's greeting to Jefferson, prompted earlier by Rush, to which Jefferson warmly responded. Thus began what historian <a href="/wiki/David_McCullough" title="David McCullough">David McCullough</a> calls "one of the most extraordinary correspondences in American history".<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">[218]</a></sup> Over the next fourteen years, the former presidents exchanged 158 letters discussing their political differences, justifying their respective roles in events, and debating the revolution's import to the world.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">[219]</a></sup> When Adams died, his last words included an acknowledgement of his longtime friend and rival: "Thomas Jefferson survives", unaware that Jefferson had died several hours before.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">[220]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">[221]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Autobiography">Autobiography</span></h3>
<p>In 1821, at the age of 77 Jefferson began writing his autobiography, in order to "state some recollections of dates and facts concerning myself."<sup id="cite_ref-Jeffbio_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffbio-233">[222]</a></sup> He focused on the struggles and achievements he experienced until July 29, 1790, where the narrative stopped short.*<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">[223]</a></sup> He excluded his youth, emphasizing the revolutionary era. He related that his ancestors came from Wales to America in the early 17th century and settled in the western frontier of the Virginia colony, which influenced his zeal for individual and state rights. Jefferson described his father as uneducated, but with a "strong mind and sound judgement." His enrollment in the College of William and Mary and election to the Continental Congress in Philadelphia in 1775 were included.<sup id="cite_ref-Jeffbio_233-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffbio-233">[222]</a></sup></p>
<p>He also expressed opposition to the idea of a privileged <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy" title="Aristocracy">aristocracy</a> made up of large land owning families partial to the King, and instead promoted "the aristocracy of virtue and talent, which nature has wisely provided for the direction of the interests of society, &amp; scattered with equal hand through all it's conditions, was deemed essential to a well ordered republic." <sup id="cite_ref-Jeffbio_233-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffbio-233">[222]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson gave his insight about people, politics, and events.<sup id="cite_ref-Jeffbio_233-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffbio-233">[222]</a></sup> The work is primarily concerned with the Declaration and reforming the government of Virginia. He used notes, letters, and documents to tell many of the stories within the autobiography. He suggested that this history was so rich that his personal affairs were better overlooked, but he incorporated a self-analysis using the Declaration and other patriotism.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">[224]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lafayette-scheffer.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Lafayette-scheffer.jpg/180px-Lafayette-scheffer.jpg" width="180" height="264" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Lafayette-scheffer.jpg/270px-Lafayette-scheffer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Lafayette-scheffer.jpg/360px-Lafayette-scheffer.jpg 2x" data-file-width="851" data-file-height="1246" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lafayette-scheffer.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<center>Lafayette in 1824</center>
portrait by Scheffer, hanging in U.S. House of Representatives</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lafayette.27s_visit">Lafayette's visit</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Visit_of_the_Marquis_de_Lafayette_to_the_United_States" title="Visit of the Marquis de Lafayette to the United States">Visit of the Marquis de Lafayette to the United States</a></div>
<p>In the summer of 1824, the <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_du_Motier,_Marquis_de_Lafayette" title="Gilbert du Motier, Marquis de Lafayette">Marquis de Lafayette</a> accepted an invitation from President James Monroe to visit the country. Jefferson and Lafayette had not seen each other since 1789. After visits to <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" class="mw-redirect" title="New York (state)">New York</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a>, and Washington, Lafayette arrived at Monticello on November 4.<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson_1970_ch11_222-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson_1970_ch11-222">[211]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson's grandson <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson_Randolph" title="Thomas Jefferson Randolph">Randolph</a> was present and recorded the reunion: "As they approached each other, their uncertain gait quickened itself into a shuffling run, and exclaiming, 'Ah Jefferson!' 'Ah Lafayette!', they burst into tears as they fell into each other's arms." Jefferson and Lafayette then retired to the house to reminisce.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">[225]</a></sup> The next morning Jefferson, Lafayette, and James Madison attended a tour and banquet at the University of Virginia. Jefferson had someone else read a speech he had prepared for Lafayette, as his voice was weak and could not carry. This was his last public presentation. After an 11-day visit, Lafayette bid Jefferson goodbye and departed Monticello.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">[226]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_days.2C_death.2C_and_burial">Final days, death, and burial</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Grave_Site.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Obelisk at Thomas Jefferson's gravesite" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Grave_Site.jpg/180px-Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Grave_Site.jpg" width="180" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Grave_Site.jpg/270px-Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Grave_Site.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Grave_Site.jpg/360px-Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Grave_Site.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="2048" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Grave_Site.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jefferson's gravesite</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jefferson's approximately $100,000 of debt weighed heavily on his mind in his final months, as it became increasingly clear that he would have little to leave to his heirs. In February 1826, he successfully applied to the General Assembly to hold a public lottery as a fund raiser.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis288_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis288-238">[227]</a></sup> His health began to deteriorate in July 1825, due to a combination of rheumatism from arm and wrist injuries, as well as intestinal and urinary disorders<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson_1970_ch11_222-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson_1970_ch11-222">[211]</a></sup> and, by June 1826, he was confined to bed.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis288_238-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis288-238">[227]</a></sup> On July 3, Jefferson was overcome by fever and declined an invitation to Washington to attend an anniversary celebration of the Declaration.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">[228]</a></sup></p>
<p><span id="Death"></span> During the last hours of his life, he was accompanied by family members and friends. On July 4 at 12:50&#160;p.m., Jefferson died at age 83 on the fiftieth anniversary of the Declaration of Independence, and just a few hours before the death of John Adams.<sup id="cite_ref-rayner428.E2.80.9329_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rayner428.E2.80.9329-240">[229]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Berstein189_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berstein189-241">[230]</a></sup> The sitting president was Adams' son <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy</a>, and he called the coincidence of their deaths on the nation's anniversary "visible and palpable remarks of Divine Favor".<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">[231]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shortly after Jefferson had died, attendants found a gold locket on a chain around his neck, where it had rested for more than forty years, containing a small faded blue ribbon which tied a lock of his wife Martha's brown hair.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">[232]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson's remains were buried at Monticello, under a self-written epitaph:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>HERE WAS BURIED THOMAS JEFFERSON, AUTHOR OF THE DECLARATION OF AMERICAN INDEPENDENCE, OF THE STATUTE OF VIRGINIA FOR RELIGIOUS FREEDOM, AND FATHER OF THE UNIVERSITY OF VIRGINIA.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">[233]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Jefferson died deeply in debt, unable to pass on his estate freely to his heirs.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">[234]</a></sup> He gave instructions in his will for disposal of his assets,<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">[235]</a></sup> including the freeing of Sally Hemings' children;<sup id="cite_ref-Meacham.2C_2012.2C_p._495_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham.2C_2012.2C_p._495-247">[236]</a></sup> but his estate, possessions, and slaves were sold at public auctions starting in 1827.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">[237]</a></sup> In 1831, Monticello was sold by <a href="/wiki/Martha_Jefferson_Randolph" title="Martha Jefferson Randolph">Martha Jefferson Randolph</a> and the other heirs.<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">[238]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political.2C_social.2C_and_religious_views">Political, social, and religious views</span></h2>
<p>Jefferson subscribed to the political ideals expounded by <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>, whom he considered the three greatest men that ever lived.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">[239]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">[240]</a></sup> He was also influenced by the writings of <a href="/wiki/Edward_Gibbon" title="Edward Gibbon">Gibbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">Hume</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Robertson_(historian)" title="William Robertson (historian)">Robertson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_St_John,_1st_Viscount_Bolingbroke" title="Henry St John, 1st Viscount Bolingbroke">Bolingbroke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montesquieu" title="Montesquieu">Montesquieu</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">[241]</a></sup> Jefferson thought that the independent <a href="/wiki/Yeoman#United_States" title="Yeoman">yeoman</a> and agrarian life were ideals of <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States" title="Republicanism in the United States">republican virtues</a>. He distrusted cities and financiers, favored decentralized government power, and believed that the tyranny that had plagued the common man in Europe was due to corrupt political establishments and <a href="/wiki/Monarch" title="Monarch">monarchies</a>. He supported efforts to disestablish the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a><sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">[242]</a></sup> wrote the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Statute_for_Religious_Freedom" title="Virginia Statute for Religious Freedom">Virginia Statute for Religious Freedom</a>, and he pressed for a <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state_in_the_United_States" title="Separation of church and state in the United States">wall of separation</a> between church and state.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">[243]</a></sup> The Republicans under Jefferson were strongly influenced by the 18th-century British <a href="/wiki/Whig_(British_political_party)" class="mw-redirect" title="Whig (British political party)">Whig Party</a>, who believed in <a href="/wiki/Limited_government" title="Limited government">limited government</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">[244]</a></sup> His Democratic-Republican Party became dominant in <a href="/wiki/First_Party_System" title="First Party System">early American politics</a>, and his views became known as <a href="/wiki/Jeffersonian_democracy" title="Jeffersonian democracy">Jeffersonian democracy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">[245]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smith2003_p_314_257-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith2003_p_314-257">[246]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_government">Society and government</span></h3>
<p>According to Jefferson's philosophy, citizens have "certain inalienable rights" and "rightful liberty is unobstructed action according to our will, within limits drawn around us by the equal rights of others."<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">[247]</a></sup> A staunch advocate of the jury system to protect people's liberties, he proclaimed in 1801, "I consider [trial by jury] as the only anchor yet imagined by man, by which a government can be held to the principles of its constitution." <sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">[248]</a></sup> Jeffersonian government not only prohibited individuals in society from infringing on the liberty of others, but also restrained itself from diminishing individual liberty as a protection against <a href="/wiki/Tyranny_of_the_majority" title="Tyranny of the majority">tyranny from the majority</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">[249]</a></sup> Initially, Jefferson favored restricted voting to those who could actually have free exercise of their reason by escaping any corrupting dependence on others. He advocated enfranchising a majority of Virginians, seeking to expand suffrage to include "yeoman farmers" who owned their own land while excluding tenant farmers, city day laborers, vagrants, most Amerindians, and women.<sup id="cite_ref-Wood220_261-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood220-261">[250]</a></sup></p>
<p>He was convinced that individual liberties were the fruit of political equality, which were threatened by arbitrary government.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">[251]</a></sup> Excesses of democracy in his view were caused by institutional corruptions rather than human nature. He was less suspicious of a working democracy than many contemporaries.<sup id="cite_ref-Wood220_261-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood220-261">[250]</a></sup> As president, Jefferson feared that the <a href="/wiki/Federalism_in_the_United_States" title="Federalism in the United States">Federalist system</a> enacted by Washington and Adams had encouraged corrupting patronage and dependence. He tried to restore a balance between the state and federal governments more nearly reflecting the <a href="/wiki/Articles_of_Confederation" title="Articles of Confederation">Articles of Confederation</a>, seeking to reinforce state prerogatives where his party was in a majority.<sup id="cite_ref-Wood220_261-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood220-261">[250]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson was steeped in the <a href="/wiki/Whigs_(British_political_party)" title="Whigs (British political party)">British Whig</a> tradition of the oppressed majority set against a repeatedly unresponsive court party in the Parliament. He justified small outbreaks of rebellion as necessary to get monarchial regimes to amend oppressive measures compromising popular liberties. In a republican regime ruled by the majority, he acknowledged "it will often be exercised when wrong".<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">[252]</a></sup> But "the remedy is to set them right as to facts, pardon and pacify them." <sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">[253]</a></sup> As Jefferson saw his party triumph in two terms of his presidency and launch into a third term under James Madison, his view of the U.S. as a continental republic and an "empire of liberty" grew more upbeat. On departing the presidency in 1809, he described America as "trusted with the destines of this solitary republic of the world, the only monument of human rights, and the sole depository of the sacred fire of freedom and self-government".<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">[254]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Democracy">Democracy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jefferson_Portrait_West_Point_by_Thomas_Sully.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Thomas Jefferson" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Jefferson_Portrait_West_Point_by_Thomas_Sully.jpg/220px-Jefferson_Portrait_West_Point_by_Thomas_Sully.jpg" width="220" height="316" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Jefferson_Portrait_West_Point_by_Thomas_Sully.jpg/330px-Jefferson_Portrait_West_Point_by_Thomas_Sully.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Jefferson_Portrait_West_Point_by_Thomas_Sully.jpg/440px-Jefferson_Portrait_West_Point_by_Thomas_Sully.jpg 2x" data-file-width="452" data-file-height="649" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jefferson_Portrait_West_Point_by_Thomas_Sully.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<center>Thomas Jefferson at age 78</center>
Portrait by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Sully" title="Thomas Sully">Thomas Sully</a> hanging at <a href="/wiki/West_Point" class="mw-redirect" title="West Point">West Point</a>, commissioned by Faculty and Cadets, 1821</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jefferson considered democracy to be the expression of society, and promoted national self-determination, cultural uniformity, and education of all males of the commonwealth.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">[255]</a></sup> He supported public education and a free press as essential components of a democratic nation.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">[256]</a></sup></p>
<p>After resigning as secretary of state in 1795, Jefferson focused on the electoral bases of the Republicans and Federalists. The "Republican" classification for which he advocated included "the entire body of landholders" everywhere and "the body of laborers" without land.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">[257]</a></sup> Republicans united behind Jefferson as vice president, with the election of 1796 expanding democracy nationwide at grassroots levels.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">[258]</a></sup> Jefferson promoted Republican candidates for local offices.<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">[259]</a></sup></p>
<p>Beginning with Jefferson's electioneering for the "revolution of 1800", his political efforts were based on egalitarian appeals.<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">[260]</a></sup> In his later years, he referred to the 1800 election "as real a revolution in the principles of our government as that of '76 was in its form", one "not effected indeed by the sword&#160;... but by the&#160;... suffrage of the people."<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">[261]</a></sup> Voter participation grew during Jefferson's presidency, increasing to "unimaginable levels" compared to the Federalist Era, with turnout of about 67,000 in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1800" title="United States presidential election, 1800">1800</a> rising to about 143,000 in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1804" title="United States presidential election, 1804">1804</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">[262]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the onset of the Revolution, Jefferson accepted <a href="/wiki/William_Blackstone" title="William Blackstone">William Blackstone</a>'s argument that property ownership would sufficiently empower voters' independent judgement, but he sought to further expand suffrage by land distribution to the poor.<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">[263]</a></sup> In the heat of the Revolutionary Era and afterward, several states expanded voter eligibility from landed gentry to all propertied male, tax-paying citizens with Jefferson's support.<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">[264]</a></sup> In retirement, he gradually became critical of his home state for violating "the principle of equal political rights"—the social right of universal male suffrage.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">[265]</a></sup> He sought a "general suffrage" of all taxpayers and militia-men, and equal representation by population in the General Assembly to correct preferential treatment of the slave-holding regions.<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">[266]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religious_views_of_Thomas_Jefferson" title="Religious views of Thomas Jefferson">Religious views of Thomas Jefferson</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Jefferson_Bible_Lined_Cover.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A leather-bound Bible" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Thomas_Jefferson_Bible_Lined_Cover.jpg/240px-Thomas_Jefferson_Bible_Lined_Cover.jpg" width="240" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Thomas_Jefferson_Bible_Lined_Cover.jpg/360px-Thomas_Jefferson_Bible_Lined_Cover.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Thomas_Jefferson_Bible_Lined_Cover.jpg/480px-Thomas_Jefferson_Bible_Lined_Cover.jpg 2x" data-file-width="717" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Jefferson_Bible_Lined_Cover.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jefferson's Bible featuring only the words of Jesus from the evangelists, in parallel Greek, Latin, French and English</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Baptized in his youth, Jefferson became a governing member of his local <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_Church_(United_States)" title="Episcopal Church (United States)">Episcopal Church</a> in Charlottesville, which he later attended with his daughters.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">[267]</a></sup> Influenced by <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">Deist</a> authors during his college years Jefferson abandoned "orthodox" <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> after his review of <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> teachings.<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">[268]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">[269]</a></sup> In 1803 he asserted, "I am Christian, in the only sense in which [Jesus] wished any one to be."<sup id="cite_ref-Randal583_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Randal583-180">[171]</a></sup> Jefferson later defined being a <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christian</a> as one who followed the simple teachings of <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a>. Jefferson compiled Jesus' biblical teachings, omitting miraculous or supernatural references. He titled the work <i>The Life and Morals of Jesus of Nazareth</i>, known today as the <i><a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Bible" title="Jefferson Bible">Jefferson Bible</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jefferson_Bible.2C_1820_281-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jefferson_Bible.2C_1820-281">[270]</a></sup> Peterson states Jefferson was a <a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">theist</a> "whose God was the Creator of the universe&#160;... all the evidences of nature testified to His perfection; and man could rely on the harmony and beneficence of His work."<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">[271]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson was firmly <a href="/wiki/Anti-clericalism" title="Anti-clericalism">anticlerical</a>, writing in "every age, the priest has been hostile to liberty&#160;... they have perverted the purest religion ever preached to man into mystery and jargon."<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">[272]</a></sup> The full letter to Horatio Spatford can be read at the National Archives.<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">[273]</a></sup> Jefferson once supported banning clergy from public office but later relented.<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">[274]</a></sup> In 1777, he drafted the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Statute_for_Religious_Freedom" title="Virginia Statute for Religious Freedom">Virginia Statute for Religious Freedom</a>. Ratified in 1786, it made compelling attendance or contributions to any state-sanctioned religious establishment illegal and declared that men "shall be free to profess&#160;... their opinions in matters of religion."<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">[275]</a></sup> The Statute is one of only three accomplishments he chose to have inscribed in the epitaph on his gravestone.<sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">[276]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">[277]</a></sup> Early in 1802, Jefferson wrote to the Danbury Connecticut Baptist Association, "that religion is a matter which lies solely between Man and his God." He interpreted the <a href="/wiki/First_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="First Amendment to the United States Constitution">First Amendment</a> as having built "a wall of <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state" title="Separation of church and state">separation between Church and State</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">[278]</a></sup> The phrase 'Separation of Church and State' has been cited several times by the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court</a> in its interpretation of the <a href="/wiki/Establishment_Clause" title="Establishment Clause">Establishment Clause</a>.</p>
<p>Jefferson donated to the <a href="/wiki/American_Bible_Society" title="American Bible Society">American Bible Society</a>, saying the <a href="/wiki/Four_Evangelists" title="Four Evangelists">Four Evangelists</a> delivered a "pure and sublime system of morality" to humanity. He thought Americans would rationally create "<a href="/wiki/Beekeeping" title="Beekeeping">Apiarian</a>" religion, extracting the best traditions of every denomination.<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">[279]</a></sup> And he contributed generously to several local denominations nearby Monticello.<sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">[280]</a></sup> Acknowledging <a href="/wiki/Organized_religion" title="Organized religion">organized religion</a> would always be factored into political life for good or ill, he encouraged reason over supernatural revelation to make inquiries into religion. He believed in a <a href="/wiki/Creator_deity" title="Creator deity">creator god</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Afterlife" title="Afterlife">afterlife</a>, and the sum of religion as loving God and neighbors. But he also controversially renounced the conventional Christian <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a>, denying Jesus' divinity as the <a href="/wiki/Son_of_God" title="Son of God">Son of God</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">[281]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-293" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-293">[282]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson's unorthodox religious beliefs became an important issue in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1800" title="United States presidential election, 1800">1800 presidential election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wood586_294-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood586-294">[283]</a></sup> Federalists attacked him as an <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheist</a>. As president, Jefferson countered the accusations by praising religion in his inaugural address and attending services at the Capitol.<sup id="cite_ref-Wood586_294-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood586-294">[283]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Banks">Banks</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Hamilton_portrait_by_John_Trumbull_1806.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Alexander_Hamilton_portrait_by_John_Trumbull_1806.jpg/150px-Alexander_Hamilton_portrait_by_John_Trumbull_1806.jpg" width="150" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Alexander_Hamilton_portrait_by_John_Trumbull_1806.jpg/225px-Alexander_Hamilton_portrait_by_John_Trumbull_1806.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Alexander_Hamilton_portrait_by_John_Trumbull_1806.jpg/300px-Alexander_Hamilton_portrait_by_John_Trumbull_1806.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2532" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Hamilton_portrait_by_John_Trumbull_1806.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Alexander Hamilton, national bank proponent and Jefferson's adversary</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jefferson distrusted government banks and opposed public borrowing, which he thought created long-term debt, bred monopolies, and invited dangerous speculation as opposed to productive labor.<sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">[284]</a></sup> In one letter to Madison, he argued each generation should curtail all debt within 19 years, and not impose a long-term debt on subsequent generations.<sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296">[285]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1791, President Washington asked Jefferson, then Secretary of State, and Hamilton, the Secretary of the Treasury, if the Congress had the authority to create a <a href="/wiki/First_Bank_of_the_United_States" title="First Bank of the United States">national bank</a>. While Hamilton believed Congress had the authority, Jefferson and Madison thought a national bank would ignore the needs of individuals and farmers, and would violate the <a href="/wiki/Tenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Tenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Tenth Amendment</a> by assuming powers not granted to the federal government by the states.<sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">[286]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson used agrarian resistance to banks and speculators as the first defining principle of an opposition party, recruiting candidates for Congress on the issue as early as 1792.<sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">[287]</a></sup> As president, Jefferson was persuaded by Secretary of Treasury Albert Gallatin to leave the bank intact, but sought to restrain its influence.<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">[288]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301">[l]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Slavery">Slavery</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson_and_slavery" title="Thomas Jefferson and slavery">Thomas Jefferson and slavery</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jefferson_slaves.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Farm Book page" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Jefferson_slaves.jpg/220px-Jefferson_slaves.jpg" width="220" height="274" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Jefferson_slaves.jpg/330px-Jefferson_slaves.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/33/Jefferson_slaves.jpg 2x" data-file-width="401" data-file-height="500" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jefferson_slaves.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jefferson's 1795 Farm Book, page 30, lists 163 slaves at Monticello.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jefferson lived in a planter economy largely dependent upon slavery, and as a wealthy landholder, used slave labor for his household, plantation, and workshops. He first recorded his slaveholding in 1774, when he counted 41.<sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302">[290]</a></sup> Over his lifetime he owned about 600 slaves; he inherited about 175 while most of the remainder were born on his plantations.<sup id="cite_ref-TJFSlaveryFAQ_303-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TJFSlaveryFAQ-303">[291]</a></sup> Jefferson purchased slaves in order to unite their families, and he sold about 110 for economic reasons, primarily slaves from his outlying farms.<sup id="cite_ref-TJFSlaveryFAQ_303-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TJFSlaveryFAQ-303">[291]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">[292]</a></sup> Many historians have described Jefferson as a benevolent slaveowner<sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">[293]</a></sup> who didn't overwork his slaves by the conventions of his time, and provided them log cabins with fireplaces, food, clothing and some household provisions, though slaves often had to make many of their own provisions. Additionally Jefferson gave his slaves financial and other incentives while also allowing them to grow gardens and raise their own chickens. The use of the whip was employed only in rare and extreme cases of fighting and stealing.<sup id="cite_ref-TJFSlaveryFAQ_303-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TJFSlaveryFAQ-303">[291]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TJFSlaveDwellings_306-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TJFSlaveDwellings-306">[294]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson once said, "My first wish is that the labourers may be well treated".<sup id="cite_ref-TJFSlaveryFAQ_303-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TJFSlaveryFAQ-303">[291]</a></sup> Jefferson did not work his slaves on Sundays and Christmas and he allowed them more personal time during the winter months.<sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">[295]</a></sup> Some scholars doubt Jefferson's benevolence,<sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">[296]</a></sup> however, noting cases of excessive slave whippings in his absence. His nail factory was also only staffed by child slaves, many of those boys became tradesmen. Burwell Colbert, who started his working life as a child in Monticello's Nailery, was later promoted to the supervisory position of butler.<sup id="cite_ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309">[297]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson felt slavery was harmful to both slave and master, but had reservations about releasing unprepared slaves into freedom and advocated gradual emancipation.<sup id="cite_ref-TJFslavery_310-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TJFslavery-310">[298]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-311" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-311">[299]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">[300]</a></sup> In 1779, he proposed gradual voluntary training and resettlement to the Virginia legislature, and three years later drafted legislation allowing owners to free their own slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">[301]</a></sup> In his draft of the Declaration of Independence, he included a section, stricken by other Southern delegates, criticizing King George III's role in promoting slavery in the colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">[302]</a></sup> In 1784, Jefferson proposed the abolition of slavery in all western U.S. territories, limiting slave importation to 15 years.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling_2000_p287_315-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling_2000_p287-315">[303]</a></sup> Congress, however, failed to pass his proposal by one vote.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling_2000_p287_315-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling_2000_p287-315">[303]</a></sup> In 1787, Congress passed the Northwest Ordinance, a partial victory for Jefferson that terminated slavery in the Northwest Territory.
